When the Arrow Rebounds: The Last Cause Principle
States the ruling that a rebound arrow killing is khata' due to the principle of attaching to the last cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a rebound arrow kills and explain the legal principle of 'akhir al-sababayn'.

Core Idea
The ruling attaches to the last cause, so a rebound arrow killing is error (khata') even if the initial shot was intentional.

In Hanafi criminal law, causation chains can break when an intervening accident occurs. This lesson examines a classic case: an arrow that misses, hits a wall, rebounds, and kills. The school's ruling hinges on the principle that legal responsibility follows the last cause in existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? Shooting an arrow that rebounds and kills. Ruling: khata' (error), not intentional ('amd).
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is not a direct text but a rational principle: the ruling is attached to the last of two causes (akhir al-sababayn). The rebound is based on hitting the wall, not the original shot.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the principle apply? The hitting of the wall is a new, intervening cause that severs the link to the original shooting. Since hitting the wall was an accident, the resulting death is also accidental.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may consider the original intent, but Hanafis emphasize the last cause. This affects the type of compensation: only diya, not qisas.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, the difference is between qisas (death penalty) for intentional killing vs. diya (blood money) for error. In this case, the killer pays diya and observes kaffarah (expiation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجلاً فأصاب حائطاً ثم رجع السهم فأصاب الرجل فهو خطأ لأنه أخطأ في أصابة الحائط ورجوع السهم مبني على إصابة الحائط لا على الرمي السابق لأنه آخر السببين والحكم يضاف إلى آخر السببين وجوداً وقد تخلل بين الرمي والإصابة الأخيرة إصابة الحائط فقطع حكم الإصابة الأخيرة على الرمي السابق
A man [shot an arrow and] it hit a wall, then the arrow rebounded and hit the man, so it is error (khata') because he erred in hitting the wall, and the rebound of the arrow is based on hitting the wall, not on the previous shot, because it [the rebound] is the last of the two causes, and the ruling is attached to the last of the two causes in existence, and the hitting of the wall intervened between the shot and the final hitting, so it severed the ruling of the final hitting from the previous shot.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning (talil). The case: a person shoots an arrow, it misses the intended target and hits a wall, then rebounds and kills a man. According to Hanafi fiqh, this is categorized as khata' (error), not 'amd (intentional). The reasoning is that the effective cause is the hitting of the wall, which caused the rebound, not the original act of shooting. In Hanafi legal theory, when two causes occur, the ruling is attached to the last one in existence. Here, the last cause is the arrow's impact on the wall, which was accidental. Therefore, the killing is a mistake, and only diya (blood money) is required, not qisas (retaliation). This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is relied upon in the madhhab.

Key Terms
آخر السببين (akhir al-sababayn): The later of two causes; the legal ruling is attached to the most proximate cause in existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an arrow hits a wall and rebounds, killing someone, what is the homicide classification in the Hanafi school?
A) 'Amd (intentional)
B) Khata' (error)
C) Shibh 'amd (semi-intentional)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The killing by a rebounding arrow is classified as khata' (error) in the Hanafi school because the ruling attaches to the last cause—the accidental hitting of the wall.

Striking with a Cloth: 'Amd Whether Wound or Death
Ruling on intentional harm with a cloth causing a head wound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that striking with a cloth causing a mudihah is 'amd regardless of outcome, and identify the key role of intent over weapon.

Core Idea
Intent makes the act 'amd, not the weapon.

In Hanafi criminal law, the classification of a blow as intentional ('amd) or accidental depends on intent and effect, not just the tool. This passage addresses a scenario where a cloth is used to strike the head, causing a mudihah. The ruling clarifies that even a soft object can produce 'amd if the striker intended harm and the blow caused injury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and result
The text describes a person wrapping a cloth and striking another's head, producing a mudihah. The result may be just the wound or death.
2
State the ruling
It is 'amd (intentional) in both cases. The Hanafi position treats it as 'amd regardless of death occurring or not.
3
Understand the reasoning
The text gives the reason: 'because he hit with what he intended, and the pains performed their function, affecting both the apparent and the internal'.
The key is the intentional strike and its effect, not the nature of the object.
4
Recognize the legal consequence
Because it is 'amd, qisas is due for the wound (if mudihah) and for death if the victim dies. The text focuses on classification, not penalty details.
5
Memorize the key phrase
فشجه موضحة فهو عمد – 'causing a mudihah, it is intentional'.
This phrase encapsulates the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو لف ثوباً فضرب به رأس إنسان فشجه موضحة فهو عمد، سواء اقتصر على الشجة أو مات لأنه أصاب ما تعمد به وقد عملت الآلام عملها أثرت في الظاهر والباطن جميعاً وقد مات من غير أن يجرح
And if he wrapped a cloth and struck the head of a person with it, causing a head wound (mudihah), then it is intentional, whether he stopped at the wound or died, because he hit with what he intended, and the pains performed their function, affecting both the apparent and the internal, and he died without being [further] wounded.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling. If a person wraps a cloth (like a turban) and strikes someone's head, causing a mudihah (a wound that exposes the skull), it is considered 'amd (intentional) regardless of whether the victim dies or only suffers the wound. The reasoning given is that the blow was delivered intentionally, and the pain from it caused both external and internal injury, even if there is no additional cutting or jabbing. This is a representative example from the Hanafi school: the key is the intentional act of striking with the object; the result (death or wound) does not change the classification. This ruling is part of the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the madhhab.

Key Terms
موضحة (mudihah): A wound that lays bare the bone of the skull.
عمد (amd): Intentional act, especially in homicide and wounding, triggering qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person wraps a cloth, strikes another's head, causing a mudihah, and the victim dies, the homicide is classified as:
A) Khat'ah (error)
B) 'Amd (intentional)
C) Shibh 'amd
D) Sabbab (causation)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, intentionally striking with a cloth causing a mudihah is 'amd, whether the victim dies or not.

Striking with Sword in Scabbard: Abu Hanifah vs Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for a strike with a sword in its scabbard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifah and Muhammad on qisas for a strike with a sword in its scabbard, and distinguish the role of intent versus the instrument's capacity.

Core Idea
The intended action, not the resulting death, determines qisas when the instrument's typical use is non-lethal.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute recorded by Imam Muhammad in his book al-Diyat. The case examines whether striking someone with a sword that is sheathed in a scabbard, which then pierces the scabbard and kills, constitutes intentional murder ('amd) or error (khata'). Understanding this disagreement reveals how Hanafi jurists weigh subjective intent against objective instrument potency.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Read the case: a man strikes another with a sword in its scabbard; the sword pierces the scabbard and kills the victim. Identify the key element: the striker intended to strike with the scabbard, not to cut with the sword.
2
Identify Abu Hanifah's position and its basis
Abu Hanifah rules no qisas because the intended act was striking with the scabbard (customarily non-lethal), and the cutting death was unintentional. This makes the killing khata' (error).
The basis: custom (urf) determines the typical use of the instrument.
3
Identify Muhammad's position and its basis
Muhammad rules qisas if the scabbard alone can kill and the striker intended to kill. The scabbard's capacity to kill, combined with intent, makes it 'amd (intentional).
Focus on the objective capability of the instrument.
4
Analyze the point of disagreement
The key issue: should the ruling be based on what the striker intended (striking) or on the capability of the instrument (scabbard could kill)? Abu Hanifah prioritizes the intended act; Muhammad gives weight to the potential lethality.
The dispute is about the prioritization of subjective intent vs. objective risk.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position and rationale
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifah's: no qisas, only diya. This is because the custom (urf) is that scabbards are not used for killing, and the intent was only to strike.
Tarjih by later authorities like al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال محمد في الديات : رجل ضرب رجلاً بسيف بغمده فخرق السيف العمدة فقتله قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى : لا قود عليه وقال محمد: إن كان الغمد يقتل لو ضرب به وحده يقتل لأن الغمد لا يقصد به إلا الضرب إذا كان يقتل به وهو قاصد إلى القتل وقد أصاب المقتل فوجب القصاص لأبي حنيفة أنه أصاب الضرب دون القتل لأن الغمد لا يقصد به إلا الضرب عادة فصورة الخظأ . أن هو يصيب خلاف ما قصد
And Muhammad said in al-Diyat: A man struck a man with a sword in its scabbard, and the sword tore through the scabbard and killed him. Abu Hanifah said: There is no retaliation upon him. And Muhammad said: If the scabbard kills if struck with it alone, then he is killed [as retaliation] because the scabbard is not intended except for striking; if it can kill with it, and he intended to kill, and he hit the fatal spot, so retaliation is due. Against Abu Hanifah: he [the striker] hit the blow but not the killing, because the scabbard is only intended for striking by custom, so it is a case of error: that he hit something other than what he intended.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded by Muhammad al-Shaybani in his book al-Diyat. The case: a person strikes another with a sword that is inside its scabbard. The force causes the sword to pierce the scabbard and kill the victim. Imam Abu Hanifah (d. 150 AH) rules that no qisas (retaliation) is due, because the intended act was striking with the scabbard (which is not typically lethal), and the sword's cutting action was unintentional — thus the killing is a mistake (khata'). Imam Muhammad (d. 189 AH) disagrees: if the scabbard alone could kill (i.e., it is heavy or hard enough), and the striker intended to kill, then qisas is due, because the object used (the scabbard) is capable of killing and the fatal result occurred. The text gives Abu Hanifah's reasoning: the striker only intended the blow with the scabbard, not the cutting death. This is a classic example of how Hanafis weigh intent versus the capacity of the instrument. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the madhhab is that of Abu Hanifah, as the later commentaries (like al-Hidayah) prefer his view. This dispute is recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah? Actually it's from Muhammad's book al-Diyat, which is part of the Nawadir or perhaps included in al-Jami' al-Kabir? The text mentions 'قال محمد في الديات' which is a separate work by Muhammad al-Shaybani, but it is considered a primary source. However, the ruling of Abu Hanifah is from the riwayah of Muhammad, thus it has weight. The authority_weight is thus within the Hanafi corpus, but not necessarily Zahir al-Riwayah in the strictest sense. It is a known ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
غمد (ghamd): Scabbard or sheath for a sword.
الضرب (al-darb): The act of striking or hitting.
القتل (al-qatl): Killing or causing death.
الخطأ (al-khata'): Error or mistake; an unintentional act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, if a person strikes another with a sword in its scabbard, and the sword pierces the scabbard and kills, what is the ruling?
A) Qisas wajib
B) Diya wajib without qisas
C) No liability
D) Both qisas and kaffarah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi criminal law, the ruling on qisas depends not only on the outcome but also on the intended act and the customary use of the instrument.

The Rationale for Compensation in Accidental Killing
Rational argument for monetary compensation in accident cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that diya is wajib in accidental killing and explain the rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī) involving qiyās on property protection.

Core Idea
When qisas is impossible, monetary compensation protects the inviolability of blood by analogy to property.

Accidental killing raises a critical legal question: how to uphold the sanctity of life when the standard punitive measure—qisas—cannot be applied due to lack of intent. The Hanafi school answers this through a rational argument: just as property rights require compensation for damage, so too does the inviolable human life demand monetary restitution to prevent blood from being wasted. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the prophetic examples that illustrate its coherence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
In accidental killing, qisas (retributive punishment) is impossible because there was no intent to kill. Without some other remedy, the victim's blood would be wasted—a grave violation of its inviolability (ḥurma). The Hanafi school thus argues that monetary compensation (ḍamān) becomes wajib as a necessity to safeguard the sanctity of blood.
The term 'ihtidār' (wasting) is key: blood is too sacred to be left unprotected.
2
Examine the qiyās (analogy) on property
The author draws an analogy: a person's property (māl) is inviolable, and if it is damaged even unintentionally, compensation is wajib. Since the human self (nafs) is even more inviolable, it follows that unintentional harm to life or limb also necessitates compensation. This is qiyās jaliyy—the illah (effective cause) is the need to preserve inviolability when restitution is otherwise impossible.
The analogy is talīl bi al-shabah: the inviolability of life is the operative cause.
3
Analyse the first illustrative example (healthy hand vs. paralyzed hand)
If a man cuts off another's healthy hand, and the cutter's own hand is paralyzed, the victim has a choice: take compensation (arsh) or cut off the paralyzed hand (which is an imperfect qisas). This shows that when full qisas is impossible, the law provides an alternative financial remedy—proving that compensation does not conflict with the concept of qisas.
This example refutes the objection that qisas and diya cannot coexist.
4
Analyse the second illustrative example (pardon by one heir)
If one of the heirs of a murder victim pardons the killer, the right to qisas is nullified for all heirs, and instead they receive diya. This demonstrates that even in intentional killing, a single pardon transforms the remedy from qisas to diya, confirming that financial compensation is a substitute when the primary right (qisas) cannot be fully exercised.
The ruling: the remaining heirs do not consent to diya, yet it becomes wajib—showing it is not based on their choice but on the impossibility of qisas.
5
Synthesise the legal principle
The overarching principle: wherever qisas is impossible or cannot be fully executed, the law defaults to monetary compensation (diya/arsh) to preserve the victim's right and the inviolability of blood. This applies in accidental killing (khaṭa'), quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-‘amd), and even in cases where qisas becomes impossible due to pardon or physical incapacity.
This principle reflects the Hanafi commitment to preventing the waste of protected interests (ḥifẓ al-maqāṣid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخطأ قلنا: وجوب الضمان في الخطأ ضرورة صون الدم عن الإهدار باعتبار أنه مال له، وهذا لأنه لما تعذر العقوبة وهو القصاص لعدم الجناية إليه لصون الدم عن الإهدار ولولا ذلك لتخلط كثير من الناس وأدى إلى التفاني ولأن النفس محترمة فلا تسقط حرمتها بعذر الخاطئ كما في المال فيجب المال صيانة لها عن الإهدار ولا يقال وجوب القصاص لا ينافي وجوب المال ولا العدول إليه من غير رضا الجاني ألا ترى أن رجلاً لو قطع يد رجل وهي صحيحة ويد القاطع شلاء فالمقطوع يده بالخيار إن شاء أخذ الأرش، وإن شاء قطع يده الشلاء. وكذا لو عفا أحد الأولياء بطل حق الباقين في القصاص ووجب لهم الدية، ولو أنه وجب بالجناية لما وجب بغير رضاهم لأنا نقول: إنما كان لهم ذلك لتعذر استيفاء حقهم كاملا.
Regarding accidental killing, we say: The obligation of compensation in accidental killing is a necessity to safeguard blood from being wasted, considering it as property. This is because when the punishment (qisas) is impossible due to the absence of intention, compensation is required to preserve blood from being wasted. Were it not for that, many people would become confused and it would lead to mutual destruction. And because the soul is inviolable, its sanctity is not nullified by the mistake of the wrongdoer, just as in property, so wealth is obligatory to protect it from being wasted. It cannot be said that the obligation of qisas does not negate the obligation of wealth, nor that it is a deviation from it without the consent of the offender. Do you not see that if a man cuts off the hand of another man, his hand being healthy, while the cutter's hand is paralyzed, the victim has the choice: if he wishes, he may take compensation (arsh), and if he wishes, he may cut off the paralyzed hand. Similarly, if one of the heirs pardons, the right of the remaining heirs to qisas is nullified and the blood money becomes obligatory for them. And if it had been obligatory due to the crime, it would not be obligatory without their consent. We say: They only have that because it is impossible to fully exact their right.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining why monetary compensation (diya/arsh) is obligatory in cases of accidental killing or injury. The author argues that when the primary punishment of qisas is not applicable due to lack of intent, financial compensation becomes necessary to prevent the victim's blood from being wasted. This is based on the principle that human life and limb are inviolable and must be protected, analogous to property. The examples illustrate that in injury cases, the victim has the option of qisas or compensation, and that pardon by one heir converts the right to qisas into a right to diya for all heirs. The reasoning refutes any objection that qisas and compensation cannot coexist by showing that they do coexist in analogous situations.

Key Terms
الخطأ (al-khaṭa'): Accidental killing where there is no intent to kill or cause harm.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability for monetary compensation, here referring to diya or arsh.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retributive punishment equivalent to the harm caused, e.g., death for murder.
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation for injuries less than death, determined by the severity.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of the deceased in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason monetary compensation becomes obligatory in accidental killing?
A) Because the killer's tribe must pay a penalty.
B) To safeguard blood from being wasted when qisas is impossible.
C) Because the victim's family demands compensation.
D) To deter future accidents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, accidental killing obligates diya (blood money) on the 'āqilah to preserve the inviolability of life when qisas cannot be applied.

Why Kaffarah Is Not Obligatory for Intentional Killing
Hanafi refutation of Shafi'i on kaffarah for intentional killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that kaffarah is not obligatory for intentional killing and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the dual nature of kaffarah.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is not for pure sins; it requires a cause that is mixed between permissible and prohibited.

When a killing is intentional, should the killer also have to perform expiation (kaffarah) to erase the sin, beyond the legal penalty? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi proof and the Shafi'i objection, then refines the Hanafi argument with deep usul reasoning. By understanding this dispute, you will grasp how the Hanafi school classifies the nature of kaffarah and why it cannot be extended by analogy to pure major sins.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: kaffarah is not obligatory for intentional killing (qatl al-'amd).
The matn simply says 'No' — meaning no expiation.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that kaffarah is wajib by qiyas (analogy) with accidental killing, and a fortiori because the sin is greater.
The Shafi'i argument is a classic qiyas al-awlawi (the more deserving analogy).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: kaffarah erases sin, and intentional killing has more sin, so it is more deserving of kaffarah.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: kaffarah oscillates between worship and punishment, so its cause must also be mixed between permissible and prohibited. Intentional killing is purely prohibited, so it cannot be a cause. The analogy with accidental killing fails because accidental is less sinful; legislating for the lesser does not imply for the greater.
The text also addresses an objection about killing game in ihram, but the commentator critiques that answer.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi does not perform kaffarah for intentional killing, while a Shafi'i does (e.g., freeing a slave or fasting two months).
This affects fatwa and practice in cases of homicide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (لا) (الكفارة أي لا تجب الكفارة بقتل العمد قال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى: تجب اعتباراً بالخطأ بل أولى لأنها شرعت تمحو الإثم وهو في العمد أكثر فكان ادعى إلى إيجابها. ولنا أن الكفارة دائرة بين العبادة والعقوبة فلا بد من أن يكون سببها أيضاً دائراً بين الخطر والإباحة لتعلق العبادة بالمباح والعقوبة بالمحظور وقتل العمد كبيرة محض فلا تناط به كسائر الكبائر مثل الزنا والسرقة والربا. قال تاج الشريعة: فإن قلت: يشكل بكفارة قتل صيد المحرم فإنه كبيرة محضة ومع هذا تجب فيه الكفارة قلت: هو جناية على المحل ولهذا لو اشترك رجلان في قتل صيد الحرم يلزم جزاء واحد ولو كان جناية الفعل لوجب جزآن والجناية على المحل يستوي فيها العمد والخطأ ا هـ. أقول: في الجواب بحث؛ أما أولاً فلأنه لا يدفع السؤال المذكور لأن مورده مضمون الدليل المزبور وهو الكفارة لا تناط بما هو كبيرة محضة لا أصل المدعي وهو أنه لا كفارة في القتل العمد، فإذا سلم كون قتل صيد الحرم كبيرة محضة يلزم أن يشكل الدليل المزبور به سواء كان في جناية الفعل أو جناية المحل، وكون الجناية على المحل يستوي فيها العمد والخطأ إنما يفيد لو ورد السؤال على أصل المدعي فإنه يمكن الجواب عنه حينئذ بأن ما قلناه في جناية الفعل دون جناية المحل وقتل صيد الحرم من قبيل الثانية دون الأولى. وأما ثانياً فلأنه قد تقرر في كتب أصول الفقه أن الكفارة جزاء الفعل من كل الوجوه لا جزاء المحل أصلاً، فلو كان قتل صيد الحرم جناية على المحل لا جناية الفعل لزم أن لا تصلح الكفارة لكون الكفارة جزاء الفعل من كل الوجوه لا جزاء المحل أصلاً، ولا يمكن قياسه على الخطأ لأنه دونه في الإثم فشرعه لدفع الأدنى لا يدل على دفع الأعلى، ولأن في قتل العمد وعيداً محكماً ولا يمكن أن يقال يرتفع المأثم فيه بالكفارة مع وجود الشدة في الوعيد بنص قاطع لا شبهة فيه، ومن ادعى ذلك كان محكماً فيه بلا دليل، ولأن الكفارة من المقدورات فلا يجوز إثباتها بالقياس على ما عرف في موضعه ولأن قوله تعالى {فجزاؤه جهنم} الآية. كل موجبه إذ هو مذكور في سياق الجزاء للشرط فتكون الزيادة عليه نسخاً ولا يجوز بالرأي.
The author, may Allah have mercy on him, said: '(No) expiation (kaffarah), i.e., expiation is not obligatory for intentional killing.' Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is obligatory, by analogy with accidental killing, rather more so, because expiation is legislated to erase sin, and sin is greater in intentional killing, so it is more deserving of being obligatory.' Our proof: Expiation oscillates between worship and punishment, so its cause must also oscillate between danger and permissibility, because worship is attached to the permissible and punishment to the prohibited. Intentional killing is a pure major sin, so it is not attached to it, like other major sins such as adultery, theft, and usury. Taj al-Shari'ah said: 'If you say: This is problematic because of the expiation for killing the game of a pilgrim (muhrim), for that is a pure major sin, yet expiation is obligatory in it. I say: That is an offense against the sanctity (of the sacred precincts), and therefore if two men share in killing game in the sanctuary, one penalty is due, whereas if it were an offense against the act, two penalties would be due. In an offense against the sanctity, intentional and accidental are equal.' I say: There is discussion in this answer. First, because it does not refute the aforementioned question, since the subject matter is the content of the aforementioned proof, namely that expiation is not attached to anything that is a pure major sin, not the original claim that there is no expiation in intentional killing. If it is conceded that killing game in the sanctuary is a pure major sin, then the aforementioned proof would be problematic, whether it is an offense against the act or against the sanctity. The fact that in an offense against the sanctity, intentional and accidental are equal would only benefit if the question were raised on the original claim, for then it could be answered by saying that what we said applies to offense against the act, not against the sanctity, and killing game in the sanctuary is of the latter type, not the former. Second, because it is established in the books of Usul al-Fiqh that expiation is a penalty for the act in every respect, not a penalty for the sanctity at all. If killing game in the sanctuary were an offense against the sanctity and not an offense against the act, then expiation would not apply, because expiation is a penalty for the act in every respect, not a penalty for the sanctity at all. It cannot be analogized to accidental killing because it is lower in sin, so its legislation to ward off the lower does not indicate warding off the higher. Also, in intentional killing there is a confirmed threat, and it cannot be said that the sin is removed by expiation despite the severity of the threat by a definitive text with no ambiguity. Whoever claims that is making an arbitrary judgment without proof. Also, expiation is among the possible matters, so it is not permissible to establish it by analogy, as is known in its place. Also, Allah's saying {so his recompense is Hell} and the rest of the verse; all its requirements are mentioned in the context of recompense for a condition, so adding to it would be abrogation, and that is not permissible by opinion.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of kaffarah (expiation) in intentional killing. The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that kaffarah is not obligatory for intentional killing. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is obligatory by analogy with accidental killing, and even more so because the sin is greater. The Hanafi response argues that kaffarah has a dual nature between worship and punishment, so its cause must also be mixed between permissible and prohibited. Intentional killing is purely prohibited (a pure major sin), so it cannot be the cause for kaffarah, just like other major sins. The text then addresses an objection regarding kaffarah for killing game while in ihram, which is also a pure major sin but has kaffarah. The response distinguishes between an offense against the act and an offense against the sanctity; the latter does not involve a pure sinful act but rather a violation of the sacred place. The commentator (author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) criticizes this response on two grounds: first, it does not resolve the objection because it still admits that killing game is a pure major sin, and second, the distinction between act and sanctity is not valid in usul because kaffarah is tied to the act, not the place. Additional arguments are given: analogy with accidental killing is invalid because it is a weaker case; the Qur'anic threat of Hell for intentional killing cannot be overridden by kaffarah; and kaffarah cannot be established by qiyas since it is an 'ibadah (act of worship). The verse {so his recompense is Hell} (Surah al-Furqan? Actually Surah al-Nisa' 4:93 or similar) shows that the punishment is Hell, so any addition would be abrogation by opinion, which is invalid.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation, a religious obligation to atone for certain sins, such as freeing a slave or fasting.
قتل العمد (qatl al-ʿamd): Intentional killing with the intent to kill using a weapon or means that typically cause death.
الخطأ (al-khaṭa'): Accidental killing without intent to kill or cause harm.
جناية المحل (jināyat al-maḥall): Offense against the sanctity of a place (e.g., the Haram) rather than against the act itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is kaffarah not obligatory for intentional killing?
A) Because the killer may be executed and cannot perform kaffarah
B) Because kaffarah is only for accidental killing and not for intentional
C) Because kaffarah requires a cause that is mixed between permissible and prohibited, and intentional killing is a pure major sin
D) Because the verse {فجزاؤه جهنم} explicitly mentions that there is no kaffarah

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is not obligatory for intentional killing in the Hanafi school because it requires a cause that is mixed between permissible and prohibited, and intentional killing is a pure major sin.

Defining Shibh al-'Amd and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Instruments
Defining shibh al-'amd and the intra-Hanafi dispute on the instrument's size

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn on the instrument used in shibh al-'amd and apply the relied-upon Hanafi position to classify a given killing scenario.

Core Idea
In shibh al-'amd, the instrument matters: Abu Hanifah holds any non-sharp object, regardless of size, while the Sahibayn consider large lethal instruments as 'amd.

This lesson examines the Hanafi definition of quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-'amd) and the critical disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifah and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The point of contention is whether the size of a non-sharp instrument matters in determining intent. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying qisas and diyah.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifah's position
Identify the ruling from the matn: shibh al-'amd is when the killer intentionally strikes with a non-sharp instrument (e.g., stick, stone), regardless of size. The consequence is sin, kaffarah, heavy diya on the 'aqilah, and no qisas.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's distinction
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differentiate based on the instrument's typical lethality: if it is large and habitually kills (e.g., large stone or log), it is 'amd with qisas; only small non-lethal instruments are shibh al-'amd.
Their view focuses on the instrument's common outcome.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn cite the Prophet's actions: crushing the heads of a Jew and a boy between stones, and the case of a woman killed with a tent pole. They argue these show that lethal instruments indicate intent.
These cases are used to analogize large instruments to sharp weapons.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifah's response
Abu Hanifah relies on the hadith 'the killed of accidental intentional killing is the killed of the whip, stick, and stone' which is general and includes all sizes. He refutes the opposing hadith: the Jew was a highway robber (hadd punishment), and the woman's hadith is considered weak due to the Bedouin's rhymed prose.
The general hadith takes precedence; doubt in intent prevents qisas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifah, killing with a large stone is shibh al-'amd (no qisas, only diya). Under the Sahibayn, it is 'amd and requires qisas.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifah's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: وشبهه وهو إن يتعمد ضربه بغير ما ذكر الإثم والكفارة على القاتل ودية مغلظة على العاقلة لا القود أي موجب القتل شبه العمد الإثم والكفارة على القاتل والدية المغلظة على العاقلة ولا يوجب القصاص. وقوله وهو أن يتعمد ضربه بغير ما ذكر أي بغير ما ذكر في العمد والذي ذكر في العمد هو المحدد وغيره هو الذي لا حد له الأدلة، وكالحجر والعصا وكل شيء ليس له حد يفرق الأجزاء، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وفي شرح الطحاوي شبه العمد عند الإمام تعمد الضرب بما ليس بسلاح في معنى السلاح في تفريق الأجزاء. قال محمد: ويكون قصده الضرب والتأديب. وقالا: إذا ضربه بحجر عظيم أو بخشبة عظيمة فهو عمد وشبه العمد أن يتعمد ضربه بما لا يقتل به غالباً. ولهما أن معنى العمدية يتقاصر باستعمال آلة لا تقتل غالباً لأنه يقصد به التأديب، وأما التي تقتل غالباً كالسيف فكان عمداً فوجب القود ألا تری أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام رض بين حجرين رأس يهودي رض رأس صبي بين حجرين، وكذا قتل المرأة التي قتلت امرأة بمسطح وهو عمود الفسطاط. ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ألا أن قتيل خطأ العمد قتيل السوط والعصا والحجر وفيه دية مغلظة مائة من الإبل منها أربعون خلفة في بطونها أولادها وبإطلاقه يتناول العصا الكبير والكلام في مثلها ولأن قضية القتل أمر مبطن لا يعرف إلا بدليل وهو استعمال الآلة القاتلة على ما بينا، وهذه الآلة لا تصلح دليلاً على قصد القتل لأنها غير موضوعة له ولا مستعملة فيه إذ لا يمكن القتل بها على غفلة منه ولا يقع القتل بها غالباً فقدمت العمدية كذلك فصار كالعصا الصغير، وهذا لأن ما يوجب القصاص وهو الآلة المحدودة لا يختلف بين الصغير منهما والكبير لأن الكل صالح للقتل لتخريب البنية ظاهراً وباطناً فكذا ما لا يوجب القصاص وجب أن يسوي بين الصغير والكبير منه حتى لا يوجب الكل القصاص لأنه غير معد للقتل ولا صالح له لعدم نقض البنية ظاهراً وكان في قصد القتل شك لما فيه من القصور والقصاص نهاية في العقوبة فلا يجب مع الشك، وما روياه رض اليودي يحتمل أن النبي الله مع العلم أن اليهودي كان قاطع الطريق إذا قتل بسوط أو عصا أو غيره بأي شيء كان يقتل به حداً، ويحتمل أنه جعله كقاطع الطريق لكونه ساعياً في الأرض بالفساد فقتله حداً كما يقتل قاطع الطريق فإن ذلك جائز أن يلحق به على ما بينا في قاطع الطريق. وأما حديث المرأة فقال عبيد بن فضيلة عن المغيرة بن شعبة: إن امرأتين ضربت إحداهما الأخرى بعمود الفسطاط فقتلتها فقضى رسول الله الله بالدية على عصبة القاتلة وقضى فيما في بطنها بغرة فقال الأعرابي: أغرم ممن لا طعم ولا شرب ولا صاح فاستهل ومثل ذلك باطل فقال: أسجع كسجع الأعرابي وفي رواية قال: هذا من إخوان الكهان من أجل سجعه. فعلم بذلك أن ما روياه غير صحيح، والذي يدل على ذلك حمل ابن مالك على زعمهم
The author, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And its like (shibh al-'amd), which is that he intentionally strikes him with other than what was mentioned (i.e., not with a sharp instrument). The sin and kaffarah are upon the killer, and a heavy blood money (diya mughallazah) upon the 'aqilah, not retaliation.' That is, the consequence of quasi-intentional killing is sin and kaffarah on the killer and heavy diya on the 'aqilah, and it does not necessitate qisas. His statement: 'which is that he intentionally strikes him with other than what was mentioned' means other than what was mentioned in intentional killing. What was mentioned in intentional killing is the sharp instrument. Other than that is what has no sharp edge, such as a stone, a stick, and anything that does not have an edge that separates parts. This is according to Abu Hanifah, may Allah have mercy on him. In Sharh al-Tahawi: Shibh al-'amd according to the Imam is intentionally striking with something that is not a weapon in the sense of a weapon that separates parts. Muhammad said: His intention is striking and discipline. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he strikes him with a large stone or a large piece of wood, it is intentional killing, and shibh al-'amd is that he intentionally strikes him with something that does not kill habitually. Their proof is that the meaning of intentionality is reduced by using an instrument that does not kill habitually, because he intends discipline. As for that which kills habitually, like the sword, it is intentional killing, so qisas is obligatory. Do you not see that the Prophet, peace be upon him, crushed the head of a Jew between two stones, and crushed the head of a boy between two stones, and similarly the killing of the woman who killed another woman with a misṭaḥ (a tent pole). And for Abu Hanifah, may Allah have mercy on him, the proof is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, the killed of accidental intentional killing is the killed of the whip, the stick, and the stone, and there is a heavy blood money of one hundred camels, forty of which are pregnant she-camels with their young in their wombs.' By its generality, it includes the large stick and the like. Also, because the matter of killing is hidden and cannot be known except through evidence, which is the use of a lethal instrument as we have explained. These instruments are not evidence for intent to kill because they are not placed for that purpose nor used for it, since killing cannot be done with them by surprise, and death does not habitually occur from them. Therefore, intentionality is not established, so it becomes like the small stick. This is because what necessitates qisas, i.e., the sharp instrument, does not differ between small and large, because all are capable of killing by damaging the structure outwardly and inwardly. Similarly, what does not necessitate qisas must be made equal between the small and large, so that none of them necessitate qisas, because they are not prepared for killing nor suitable for it due to lack of apparent destruction of the structure, and there is doubt in the intent to kill due to its deficiency, and qisas is extreme punishment, so it is not imposed with doubt. As for the hadith they narrated about crushing the Jew, it is possible that the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew that the Jew was a highway robber, so when he killed with a whip, stick, or anything, he was killed as a punishment (hadd). It is also possible that he treated him like a highway robber because he was corrupting the earth, so he killed him as a punishment just as a highway robber is killed, for that is permissible to apply analogy as we have explained in the chapter on highway robbers. As for the hadith of the woman, Ubayd ibn Fudaylah narrated from al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah: Two women, one struck the other with a tent pole and killed her. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, decreed diyah on the 'aqilah of the killer and decreed a ghurrah for the fetus in her womb. A Bedouin said: 'Should I be liable for one who neither ate nor drank, nor cried out?' The Prophet said: 'Are you speaking rhymed prose like the Bedouin?' In another narration, he said: 'This is from the brothers of the soothsayers because of his rhyming.' Thus it is known that what they narrated is not authentic, and evidence for that is that Ibn Malik carried it according to their claim.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines shibh al-'amd (quasi-intentional killing) and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the criteria. According to the matn, shibh al-'amd is when the killer intentionally strikes the victim with an instrument that is not sharp (like a stick or stone), and the killing results. The ruling: sin and kaffarah on the killer, heavy diya on the 'aqilah, and no qisas. Abu Hanifah holds that any instrument that is not sharp (does not separate body parts) falls under shibh al-'amd, regardless of size, because such instruments are not typically lethal and do not indicate intent to kill. The Sahibayn distinguish: if the instrument is large and likely to kill (e.g., a large stone or log), it is 'amd (intentional) and carries qisas; only if the instrument is not habitually lethal is it shibh al-'amd. Each side uses hadith evidence. Abu Hanifah cites the hadith mentioning 'whip, stick, and stone' as examples of shibh al-'amd without distinction of size. The Sahibayn cite the Prophet's actions of crushing heads with stones as examples of intentional killing with such instruments. The commentator defends Abu Hanifah's position by analyzing the hadith: the Jew and the woman cases are explained away as exceptional (hadd punishment or not authentic). The key principle is that qisas requires certainty of intent, and when there is doubt (as with non-lethal instruments), qisas is not imposed.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-ʿamd): Quasi-intentional killing where the killer intended the act but not death, using an instrument not typically lethal.
المحدد (al-muḥaddad): A sharp instrument that cuts or separates body parts, e.g., sword, knife.
دية مغلظة (diyah mughallaẓah): Increased blood money, consisting of 100 camels with specific qualities (40 pregnant), for shibh al-'amd.
العاقلة (al-ʿāqilah): The paternal relatives (asabah) of the killer who are liable to pay diya in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, what is the ruling if a person kills another with a large stone?
A) It is 'amd and requires qisas
B) It is shibh al-'amd with heavy diya and no qisas
C) It is accidental killing with ordinary diya
D) It is excusable with no penalty

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifah's position that any non-sharp instrument, regardless of size, constitutes shibh al-'amd is relied upon in fatwa, provided there is no clear intent to kill.

Shibh al-'Amd: Definition and Basic Ruling
Defines shibh al-'amd and its legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shibh al-'amd, distinguish it from 'amd using the tool criterion, and state the ruling (sin, kaffarah, heavy diyah on 'aqila, no qisas).

Core Idea
Striking with a blunt tool without intent to kill is shibh al-'amd, not 'amd.

In Hanafi criminal law, homicide is classified based on intent and the tool used. This lesson examines shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional) as defined in Al-Bahr al-Raiq. Understanding this category is crucial for determining consequences—expiation, blood money, and absence of retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
Shibh al-'amd is defined as intentionally striking with a tool that is not sharp (not muhaddad), like a stone or stick.
This distinguishes it from 'amd which uses a sharp tool.
2
State the ruling components
The killer incurs sin (ithm), must perform expiation (kaffarah), and heavy blood money (diyah mughallazah) is payable by the 'aqila; there is no qisas.
Note the distinct liability: kaffarah on killer, diyah on 'aqila.
3
Distinguish from 'amd
The sharp tool (muhaddad) indicates intent to kill ('amd), while the blunt tool indicates shibh al-'amd.
Tool type is the objective criterion in Hanafi law.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance
Imam Muhammad adds that the killer's intention is only to strike and discipline, not kill, clarifying subjective intent.
This does not change the ruling but reinforces the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات وهو إن يتعمد ضربه بغير ما ذكر الإثم والكفارة على القاتل ودية مغلظة على العاقلة لا القود أي موجب القتل شبه العمد الإثم والكفارة على القاتل والدية المغلظة على العاقلة ولا يوجب القصاص. وقوله وهو أن يتعمد ضربه بغير ما ذكر أي بغير ما ذكر في العمد والذي ذكر في العمد هو المحدد وغيره هو الذي لا حد له الآلة، وكالحجر والعصا وكل شيء ليس له حد يفرق الأجزاء، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وفي شرح الطحاوي شبه العمد عند الإمام تعمد الضرب بما ليس بسلاح في معنى السلاح في تفريق الأجزاء. قال محمد : ويكون قصده الضرب والتأديب.
Book of Homicide. It is that he intentionally strikes him with other than what was mentioned—sin and expiation upon the killer, and heavy blood money upon the 'aqila, not retaliation. That is, the cause of killing that is shibh al-'amd: sin and expiation upon the killer, and heavy diyah upon the 'aqila, and it does not necessitate qisas. And his statement: 'That he intentionally strikes him with other than what was mentioned' means other than what was mentioned in 'amd. And what was mentioned in 'amd is the sharp tool, and other than it is that which has no edge that separates parts, like a stone and a stick and everything that has no edge that separates parts. This is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: Shibh al-'amd according to the Imam is intentionally striking with something that is not a weapon in the sense of a weapon in separating parts. Muhammad said: And his intention is only striking and disciplining.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage defines shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional homicide) and outlines its ruling. The key distinction from 'amd (intentional) is the type of tool used: a sharp tool (محدد) that can cut parts of the body indicates 'amd, while a blunt tool (non-sharp) like a stone or stick indicates shibh al-'amd. The ruling for shibh al-'amd is that the killer incurs sin (إثم) and must perform expiation (كفارة), and the heavy blood money (دية مغلظة) is payable by the 'aqila (the killer's male paternal relatives). There is no qisas (retaliation). Abu Hanifa's definition is given, with Muhammad adding that the intention is only to strike and discipline, not kill.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): A category of homicide where the killer intends the act (striking) but does not intend to kill, using a tool that is not typically lethal.
المحدد (al-muhaddad): A sharp tool that can cut or separate body parts, like a sword or knife.
الآلة (al-ala): The tool used in the homicide, whether sharp or blunt.
القود (al-qawad): Retaliation (qisas) for intentional homicide.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation, a religious penalty usually involving fasting or charity, required for certain sins.
الدية المغلظة (al-diyah al-mughallazah): Heavy blood money, greater than the standard diyah, payable in shibh al-'amd.
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The male paternal relatives of the killer who are collectively responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental or semi-intentional homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The killer's intention to kill vs. not to kill
B) The type of tool used (sharp vs. blunt)
C) The amount of blood money paid
D) The number of strokes

Key Takeaway
Shibh al-'amd is defined by the use of a non-sharp tool, and its penalty excludes qisas, involving kaffarah and heavy diyah on the 'aqila.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Large Tools in Shibh al-'Amd
Discusses the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether large stones or sticks constitute 'amd or shibh al-'amd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when a large blunt tool constitutes 'amd versus shibh al-'amd and explain the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The size of a blunt tool does not change its classification: if not usually deadly, it is shibh al-'amd even if large.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether using a very large stone or stick should be considered intentional killing ('amd) or semi-intentional (shibh al-'amd). While the Sahibayn argue that a large tool capable of killing is analogous to a sword, Abu Hanifa maintains that the decisive factor is the nature of the tool—blunt vs. sharp—not its size. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the legal criteria for qisas and diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting positions
Abu Hanifa says large blunt tool = shibh al-'amd; Sahibayn say it = 'amd.
2
Analyze the proof for each side
Sahibayn use qiyas to a sword and prophetic incidents; Abu Hanifa uses the general hadith and the tool's lack of deadly nature.
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
Abu Hanifa reinterprets the Jew's case as a ḥadd punishment and questions the authenticity of the woman's case.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, no qisas (diyah and kaffarah); under Sahibayn, qisas possible.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view.

وقالا : إذا ضربه بحجر عظيم أو بخشبة عظيمة فهو عمد وشبه العمد أن يتعمد ضربه بما لا يقتل به غالباً. ولهما أن معنى العمدية يتقاصر باستعمال آلة لا تقتل غالباً لأنه يقصد به التأديب، وأما التي تقتل غالباً يحتمل أن النبي الله مع العلم أن اليهودي كان قاطع الطريق إذا قتل بسوط أو عصا أو غيره بأي شيء كان يقتل به حداً، ويحتمل أنه جعله كقاطع الطريق لكونه ساعياً في الأرض بالفساد فقتله حداً كما يقتل قاطع الطريق فإن ذلك جائز أن يلحق به على ما بينا في قاطع الطريق. وأما حديث المرأة فقال عبيد بن فضيلة عن المغيرة بن شعبة : إن امرأتين ضربت إحداهما الأخرى بعمود الفسطاط فقتلتها فقضى رسول الله الله بالدية على عصبة غير صحيح، والذي يدل على ذلك حمل ابن مالك على زعمهم القود والخطأ. أن شخصاً ظنه صيداً أو حربياً فإذا هو مسلم أو عرضاً وهو يرمي فإنهم قالوا قال حمل ابن مالك : كنت بين بنتي امرأتي فضربت إحداهما الأخرى And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he strikes him with a large stone or a large piece of wood, it is 'amd. And shibh al-'amd is that he intentionally strikes him with something that does not usually kill. Their proof: The meaning of intentionality decreases with using a tool that does not usually kill, because he intends discipline. As for that which usually kills, like the sword, it is 'amd, so qisas is obligatory. Do you not see that the Prophet, peace be upon him, crushed between two stones the head of a Jew? He crushed the head of a boy between two stones. Similarly, the killing of the woman who killed a woman with a misṭah, which is a tent pole. And for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, is the Prophet's statement: 'Indeed, the one killed by mistake of 'amd is the one killed by the whip, the stick, and the stone, and for it is a heavy blood-money of one hundred camels, of which forty are pregnant with their offspring.' Its generality includes the large stick, and the discussion is similar. And because the case of killing is a hidden matter not known except by evidence, which is the use of a deadly tool as we explained. And this tool is not suitable as evidence for intent to kill, because it is not placed for that nor used for it, since killing cannot be done with it from his being unaware, and it does not usually cause death. So intentionality is weakened, thus it becomes like the small stick. This is because what entails qisas, which is the pointed tool, does not differ between its small and large, because all are suitable for killing by destroying the structure outwardly and inwardly. Similarly, what does not entail qisas must be equal between its small and large, so that the large one also does not entail qisas, because it is not prepared for killing nor suitable for it due to the lack of destruction of the structure outwardly. And there is doubt in the intent to kill because of its weakness, and qisas is the extreme in punishment, so it is not necessary with doubt. And what they narrated about crushing the Jew: it is possible that the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew that the Jew was a highway robber, and when he killed him with a whip, stick, or other, by any means he killed him as a ḥadd punishment. And it is possible that he (the Prophet) made him like a highway robber because he was causing corruption in the land, so he killed him as a ḥadd punishment, just as the highway robber is killed, because that is permissible, as we explained regarding the highway robber. As for the hadith of the woman, 'Ubayd ibn Fudayla narrated from al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba: Two women fought, and one struck the other with a tent pole and killed her. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, ruled that the blood-money be paid by the 'aṣaba of the killer, and for the fetus in her belly he ruled a ghurra. Then a Bedouin said: 'Should I pay compensation for one who neither ate nor drank nor cried out and whose like is invalid?' He (the Prophet) said: 'Rhyming prose like the rhyming prose of the Bedouin.' In another narration: 'He is from among the brothers of the soothsayers because of his rhyming prose.' Thus it is known that what they narrated is not authentic. Supporting that is what Ḥammāl ibn Mālik allegedly said about qisas and error: that a person thought him to be game or a combatant, but then he turned out to be Muslim or a protected person. People said: Ḥammāl ibn Mālik said: 'I was between the two wives of my two women, and one struck the other with a misṭah and killed her and her fetus. So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, ruled for the fetus a ghurra and that she be killed for it.' That is how they narrated it. Ibn al-Musayyib said from Abu Salama from Abu Hurayra: Two women from Hudhayl fought, and one struck the other with a stone and killed her and what was in her belly. They brought the case to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he ruled that the blood-money of her fetus be a slave, and he ruled the blood-money of the woman on her 'āqila, and her son inherited her. Ḥammāl ibn Mālik ibn al-Nābigha said: 'O Messenger of Allah, should I pay compensation for one who neither drank, nor ate, nor spoke, nor cried out? And the like of that is invalid.' He, peace be upon him, said: 'He is from among the brothers of the soothsayers.' This is the well-known narration from Ḥammāl ibn Mālik. So how can his opposite be authentic? Then there is no difference with Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, between dying from a single blow and dying from successive blows until death. All of that is shibh al-'amd and does not necessitate qisas. And they differed regarding his (Abu Hanifa's) statement concerning successive blows (al-muwālāt).
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The Sahibayn argue that if the tool is large enough to be deadly (like a huge rock), it should be considered 'amd, because it is analogous to a sword. They support their view with apparent cases from the Prophet's practice. Abu Hanifa, however, relies on the general wording of the hadith: 'the one killed by the whip, stick, and stone'—which includes large sticks and stones—and argues that the nature of the tool (blunt vs. sharp) is what matters, not its size. He also reinterprets the evidence of the Sahibayn by suggesting that the Jew was killed as a ḥadd punishment for highway robbery, not as retaliation, and that the woman's case is not authentically transmitted. The passage concludes with Abu Hanifa's position that even multiple strokes do not change the ruling; it remains shibh al-'amd.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Killing with a blunt tool that is not usually deadly; the killer intends the act but not death.
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional killing with a sharp, deadly weapon, requiring qisas.
الموالاة (al-muwālāt): Successive blows administered to the victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'amd
B) shibh al-'amd
C) khata'
D) depends on the number of blows

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that large blunt tools do not become 'amd; the tool's normal use, not its size, determines the classification.

Successive Strikes and Lethal Methods: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute over classification of homicide methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between 'amd and shibh al-'amd in cases of successive strikes and lethal methods, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Intent inferred from repeated deadly acts – Shafi'i sees 'amd, Abu Hanifa insists on shibh al-'amd.

This passage addresses a key dispute in Hanafi-Shafi'i jurisprudence regarding when homicide qualifies as intentional ('amd) vs quasi-intentional (shibh al-'amd). The classification determines whether qisas or diyah applies. The text presents the Shafi'i view on muwalat and then an intra-Hanafi difference on other lethal methods.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate what Abu Hanifa holds regarding muwalat and methods like drowning: each remains shibh al-'amd.
The tool used is not inherently deadly; the killer's intent is assumed to be discipline, not death.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that muwalat makes it 'amd. Also note that the Sahibayn consider drowning/throwing as 'amd.
This is both inter-madhhab (Shafi'i) and intra-Hanafi (Sahibayn) opposition.
3
Examine the opponent's proof
The opponent argues that multiple blows or methods that certainly cause death demonstrate clear intent to kill.
The apparent basis is the virtual certainty of death.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that such methods can be used without lethal intent (e.g., disciplining), so doubt remains; qisas is blocked by doubt.
The Hanafi principle: doubt in intent precludes qisas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Shafi'i, qisas applies; under Hanafi, diyah and kaffarah only.
This practical difference is the crux of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : يصير عمداً بها فوجب القصاص. ولو ألقاه من جبل أو سطح أو غرقه في الماء أو حقنه حتى مات كان ذلك شبه عمد عنده وعندهما عمد
Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It becomes 'amd by means of it (successive blows), so qisas becomes obligatory. And if he threw him from a mountain or a roof or drowned him in water or poured water into him until he died, that is shibh al-'amd according to him (Abu Hanifa) and 'amd according to the two (Sahibayn).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if someone strikes another repeatedly (muwalat) until death, it clearly shows intent to kill, making it 'amd and thus requiring qisas. Abu Hanifa, however, maintains that it remains shibh al-'amd because the tool was not inherently deadly; the multiple blows do not change the category. The text also notes a related intra-Hanafi difference: for throwing from a height, drowning, or force-drinking water, Abu Hanifa treats them as shibh al-'amd, while the Sahibayn consider them 'amd. The passage is brief but highlights the core difference in juristic reasoning.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwālāt): Successive strikes or continuous actions leading to death.
الغرقه (al-gharq): Drowning as a method of killing.
الحقنه (al-ḥaqnah): Force-feeding or pouring water into someone's mouth until death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, what happens when someone strikes another repeatedly until death?
A) It remains shibh al-'amd
B) It becomes 'amd and qisas applies
C) It is considered khata'
D) The killer is excused

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, successive blows and methods like drowning remain shibh al-'amd unless the killer uses a sharp weapon, because doubt in intent precludes qisas.

Defining Accidental Killing (Khata'): Two Types and Their Ruling
Commentary defining khata' in qatl and its two subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of accidental killing (khata') and explain the corresponding Hanafi ruling of wajib kaffarah and diyah on the 'aqilah.

Core Idea
Accidental killing has two subtypes: error in intention and error in action; both require expiation and blood money on the tribe.

In Hanafi fiqh, accidental killing (khata') is a distinct category from intentional killing. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines two subtypes of khata'—error in intention and error in action—and establishes the ruling: both require kaffarah (expiation) and diyah (blood money) payable by the killer's 'aqilah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the law of unintended homicide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category
The passage defines khata' (accidental killing) and its subtypes. Note that it is a distinct category from 'amd (intentional) and shibh al-'amd (quasi-intentional).
2
Extract the two subtypes
Error in intention (khata' fi al-qasd): the killer intended the action but was mistaken about the target (e.g., thinking a Muslim is a combatant). Error in action (khata' fi al-fi'l): the killer intended a permissible act (like shooting at a target) but accidentally hit a person.
The passage also includes a third scenario: a sleeping person turning over and killing someone, which is treated like error because the action is real but unintentional.
3
State the ruling for each subtype
Both subtypes carry the same ruling: wajib kaffarah (freeing a slave or fasting two months) and diyah (blood money) payable by the 'aqilah (the killer's paternal relatives).
4
Cite the evidence
The ruling is based on Quran 4:92: 'freeing a believing slave and a compensation delivered to his family' and the practice of 'Umar (ra) which became ijma'.
5
Distinguish from other killings
Intentional killing ('amd) requires qisas (retaliation). Quasi-intentional (shibh al-'amd) requires diyah on the killer's own wealth, not the 'aqilah. Accidental killing requires diyah on 'aqilah and kaffarah on the killer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأصاب أدمياً وما جرى بمجراه كالنائم إذا انقلب على رجل فقتله الكفارة والدية على العاقلة والقتل بسبب كحافر البئر وواضع الحجر في غير ملكه الدية على العاقلة لا عرضاً فأصاب أدمياً وما جرى بمجراه كالنائم إذا انقلب على رجل فقتله الكفارة والدية على العاقلة) قوله وهو أن يرمي شخصاً إلى آخره تفسير لنفس الخطأ فإنه على نوعين: خطأ في القصد وخطأ في الفعل. وقد تبين النوعين بقوله وهو أن يرمي شخصاً ظنه صيداً أو حربياً فإذا هو مسلم» تفسير للخطأ في القصد لا في الفعل حيث أصاب ما رمى وإنما أخطأ في القصد أي الظن حيث ظن المسلم حربياً والآدمي صيداً. وقوله أو عرضاً فأصاب آدمياً» هذا بيان للخطأ في الفعل دون القصد فيكون معذوراً. أقول : في عبارة الشارح والمصنف هنا تسامح فإنه قال في تفسير الخطأ في القصد وهو أن يرمي شخصاً يظنه صيداً إلى آخره. وقال في تفسير الخطأ في الفعل وهو أن يرمي عرضاً فيصيب آدمياً. ولا يخفى أن كل واحد من نوعي الخطأ . منحصر فيما ذكره في تفسيره بل الذي ذكره في تفسير كل واحد منهما جزء من جزئياته فكان منه جداً فلم يصلح لأن يكون تفسيراً له فكان الظاهر أن يقال في كل واحد منهما وهو غير في أخص نحو أن يرمي إشارة إلى العموم كما تداركه صاحب الوقاية حيث قال : وفي الخطأ قصداً كرميه مسلماً ظنه صيداً أو حربياً وفعلاً كرميه عرضاً فأصاب آدمياً ا.هـ. ثم إن صدر الشريعة قال في شرح الوقاية : الخطأ :ضربان خطأ في القصد وخطأ في الفعل. فالخطأ الذي في الفعل إن يقصد فعلاً فيصدر منه فعل آخر كما إذا رمى الغرض فأخطأ فأصاب غيره هذا . الخطأ هو الفعل. وأما الخطأ في القصد هو . أن لا يكون الخطأ في الفعل وإنما يكون الخطأ. في قصده، فإن قصد بهذا الفعل حربياً لكن أخطأ في ذلك القصد وهو الغرض حيث لم يكن قصده ا هـ. ورد عليه صاحب الإصلاح والإيضاح حيث قال : الخطأ في الفعل أن لا يصدر عنه الفعل الذي قصده بل فعل آخر وليس كذلك فإنه إذا رمى عرض رضاً فأصابه ثم رجع عنه أو تجاوز إلى ما وراءه فأصاب رجلاً يتحقق الخطأ في الفعل والشرط المذكور ههنا مفقود في الصورتين .. ثم إنه من وجه آخر حيث اعتبر القصد فيه وذلك غير لازم فإذا سقط من يده خشبة أو لبنة فقتل رجلاً يتحقق الخطأ في الفعل ولا قصد فيه ا هـ . وقول المؤلف عرض رضاً» هذا معطوف على قيد وظاهره أن الرمي معتبر في الخطأ في الفعل وليس كذلك فإنه لو سقط منه خشبة أو لبنة فقتل رجلاً هذا خطأ في الفعل ولا رمي. وقوله كنائم انقلب على رجل تفسير لما جرى مجرى الخطأ لأن هذا ليس بخطأ حقيقة ولما وجد فعله حقيقة وجب عليه ما أتلفه كفعل الطفل فجعله كاخطأ لأنه محذور كالمخطئ، وإنما كان حكم المخطئ ما ذكره لقوله تعالى فيه فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة ودية مسلمة إلى أهله ﴾ [النساء : ۹۲] وقد قضى به عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه في ثلاث سنين بمحضر من الصحابة من غير نكير فقصار إجماعاً . أخطأ
So he hit a human and what runs in its course like a sleeping person who turns over on a man and kills him: expiation and blood money on the 'aqilah. And killing by cause like the digger of a well and placer of a stone in other than his property: blood money on the 'aqilah, not accidentally he hit a human and what runs in its course like a sleeping person who turns over on a man and kills him: expiation and blood money on the 'aqilah. His saying 'that he shoots a person...' etc. is an explanation of error itself, for it is of two types: error in intention and error in action. And the two types are clarified by his saying 'that he shoots a person he thinks is game or a combatant, and lo he is a Muslim' – explanation of error in intention not in action, where he hit what he shot, but erred only in intention i.e. opinion, where he thought the Muslim was a combatant and the human was game. And his saying 'or accidentally he hit a human' – this is an explanation of error in action without intention, so he is excused. I say: in the expression of the commentator and the author here is laxity. For he said in explaining error in intention: 'that he shoots a person he thinks is a game' etc. And he said in explaining error in action: 'that he shoots accidentally and hits a human.' And it is not hidden that each of the two types of error is confined to what he mentioned in its explanation, rather what he mentioned in explaining each of them is a part of its particulars, so it was very [insufficient] and not suitable as an explanation. So it would have been apparent to say about each of them: 'and it is other than the most specific, like shooting indicating generality', as the author of al-Wiqayah remedied when he said: 'And in error intentionally, like his shooting a Muslim thinking him game or a combatant; and in action, like his shooting accidentally and hitting a human.' Then Sadr al-Shari'ah said in Sharh al-Wiqayah: 'Error is two types: error in intention and error in action. Error in action is that he intends an action but a different action occurs from him, as when he shoots at a target and misses and hits someone else. This is error in action. As for error in intention, it is that there is no error in action, but the error is in his intention. For he intended by this action a combatant but erred in that intention, and it was the target where his intention was not [correct].' The author of al-Islah wa al-Idah objected to him, saying: 'Error in action is that the action he intended does not occur from him, but another action. And it is not as he said, because if he shoots accidentally and hits him, then returns from it or goes beyond to what is behind it and hits a man, error in action is realized, but the condition mentioned here is absent in both pictures. Then from another aspect, he considered intention in it, which is not necessary. For if a piece of wood or a brick falls from his hand and kills a man, error in action is realized with no intention in it.' The author's saying 'accidentally' is linked to a restriction, and apparently shooting is considered in error in action, but it is not so. For if a piece of wood or a brick falls from him and kills a man, this is error in action with no shooting. And his saying 'like a sleeping person who turns over on a man' is an explanation of what runs in the course of error, because this is not truly error, but since his action [of turning] is real, what he destroyed is due from him like the action of a child, so it is made like error because he is excused like the mistaken person. And the ruling of the mistaken person is that which was mentioned due to His statement, exalted be He: 'freeing a believing slave and a compensation delivered to his family' [Quran 4:92], and 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, judged by it in three years in the presence of the Companions without disapproval, so it became consensus.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling type from the commentary on the definition of error (khata') in killing. The author explains that accidental killing (khata') has two subtypes: error in intention (khata' fi al-qasd) and error in action (khata' fi al-fi'l). In error in intention, the killer aimed correctly but was mistaken about the target's identity (e.g., thinking a Muslim is a combatant or game). In error in action, the killer intended a permissible act (like shooting at a target) but accidentally hit a person. The commentator critiques earlier definitions, noting that they only gave examples rather than comprehensive definitions. He prefers the approach of the author of al-Wiqayah, who gave general descriptions. Then he discusses the ruling: both types of accidental killing require expiation (kaffarah: freeing a believing slave or fasting two months) and blood money (diyah) on the 'aqilah (the killer's tribal group), based on Quran 4:92 and the consensus of the Companions. The case of a sleeping person turning over and killing someone is treated like error because the action is real but unintentional, so the same ruling applies.

Key Terms
خطأ (khata'): Accidental killing, divided into error in intention and error in action.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the victim's family in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The killer's paternal relatives (identified by Hanafi fiqh) who are responsible for paying diyah in accidental and quasi-intentional killings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Error in weapon and error in aim
B) Error in intention and error in action
C) Error of the sleeper and error of the shooter
D) Error in person and error in place

Key Takeaway
Accidental killing in Hanafi fiqh comprises error in intention and error in action, both entailing kaffarah and diyah on the 'aqilah.

The Hanafi Commentary on Defining Khata': A Critique of Insufficient Definitions
Commentary critique of khata' definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi critique of the definition of khata' and identify the more comprehensive definition from al-Wiqayah.

Core Idea
A proper legal definition must be comprehensive; citing only examples is insufficient for a technical term.

This passage not only defines the types of accidental killing but also contains a methodological critique of the definitions in the base text. The commentator points out that the earlier definitions only gave particulars, not a true definition. This lesson examines that critique, the alternative proposed by the author of al-Wiqayah, and the ensuing discussion among Hanafi jurists about what constitutes a valid definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
The author and commentator's earlier definitions of error in intention and error in action are criticized for being too narrow—they only provide specific examples (shooting at game or accidentally hitting).
2
Understand the critique
The definitions are not generic; they are particular instances. A true legal definition ('tafsir') should encompass all cases, not just examples.
The commentator says each definition is 'a part of its particulars' (جزء من جزئياته) and thus insufficient.
3
Examine the proposed correction
The author of al-Wiqayah provided a more general formulation: 'error intentionally, like shooting a Muslim thinking him a combatant; error in action, like shooting accidentally and hitting a human.' The 'like' (الكاف) indicates example, not exhaustive definition.
This formulation is considered superior because it allows for other similar cases.
4
Analyze Sadr al-Shari'ah's definition and its refutation
Sadr al-Shari'ah defined error in action as intending one action but another occurring. The author of al-Islah wa al-Idah objects, giving counterexamples: shooting accidentally and then hitting, or a falling brick with no intended action at all.
5
Conclude on proper definitional method
A proper definition must cover all instances, including cases without any intended action (like a falling object) or without shooting. The critique emphasizes the need for general conditions rather than mere examples.

Source Text

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the flaw in the original definition of khata' fi al-fi'l?
A) It ignored the case of a sleeping person
B) It only provided examples, not a comprehensive definition
C) It included intention where none existed
D) It wrongly cited the Quran

Key Takeaway
A valid fiqh definition must be comprehensive; mere examples are insufficient.
