Shibh 'Amd in the Soul vs. Other Injuries
Ruling that shibh 'amd in life is considered 'amd for other than life

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that shibh 'amd in the soul is treated as 'amd for injuries less than death, and prove it using the hadith of al-Rubayyi'.

Core Idea
What is shibh 'amd for the soul becomes 'amd for the limb—the same slap that kills is qisas-worthy if it breaks a tooth.

The Hanafi school distinguishes shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional) for killing from the same act when it injures a body part below the soul. This lesson unpacks the rule and its proof from the hadith of al-Rubayyi', showing how the Prophet's order for qisas on a tooth establishes that no shibh 'amd exists for limbs.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the key claim: shibh 'amd in the soul (i.e., quasi-intentional killing) is considered 'amd (intentional) for injuries less than death.
This is the central legal principle of the passage.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that for injuries other than death, there is no restriction to a particular tool; therefore shibh 'amd cannot be imagined there, unlike the soul where a tool is definitive.
Shibh 'amd for the soul is defined by use of a non-deadly tool.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of al-Rubayyi': a slap broke a tooth, and the Prophet ordered qisas for the tooth.
Recorded in Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The slap would be shibh 'amd if it killed, but the Prophet still ordered qisas for the tooth, proving that the same act is 'amd for the limb.
The operative difference: no qisas for life, but qisas for the tooth.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a person slaps another and breaks a tooth, qisas is wajib; if the slap kills, only diyah is due.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from schools that may treat both as intentional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبه العمد في النفس عمد فيما سواها لأن إتلاف ما دون النفس لا يختصر بآلة دون الة فلا يتصور فيه شبه العمد بخلاف النفس على ما بينا والذي يدلك على هذا ما روي عن أنس ابن مالك أن عمة الربيع لطمت جارية فكسرت ثنيتها فطلبوا اليهم العفو فأبوا والأرش فأبوا إلا القصاص واختصموا إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فأمر رسول الله إلا القصاص فقال أنس بن النضر : أتكسر ثنية الربيع والذي بعثك بالحق نبياً لا تكسر ثنيتها فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : يا أنس كتاب الله القصاص فرضي القوم فعفوا فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن من عباد الله من لو أقسم على الله لأبره. ووجه دلالته على ما نحن فيه أننا علمنا أن اللطمة لو أتت على النفس لا توجب القصاص ورأيناها فيما دون النفس قد أوجبته بحكمه عليه الصلاة والسلام فثبت بذلك أن ما كان من النفس شبه عمد فهو عمد فيما دونها ولا يتصور أن يكون شبه عمد والله أعلم .
And quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd) in the soul is intentional ('amd) in what is other than it, because destruction of what is less than the soul is not confined to a particular tool, so shibh 'amd cannot be conceived in it, unlike the soul, as we have explained. And what indicates this is what is related from Anas ibn Malik that the aunt of al-Rubayyi' slapped a slave-girl and broke her incisor tooth. They asked for forgiveness, but they refused, and for the blood money, but they refused, and insisted on qisas. They brought the case to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and the Messenger of Allah ordered qisas. Anas ibn al-Nadr said: 'Will you break the incisor of al-Rubayyi'? By Him who sent you with the truth, do not break her incisor.' The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'O Anas, the book of Allah is qisas.' Then the people were satisfied and forgave. Then the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Among the servants of Allah are those who, if they swear by Allah, He fulfills it.' And the indication of this for what we are in is that we know that if the slap had led to the soul (death), it would not have necessitated qisas, and we see that in what is less than the soul, it has necessitated it by his ruling, peace be upon him. So it is established that what is shibh 'amd regarding the soul is 'amd regarding what is less than it, and shibh 'amd cannot be conceived. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof type discussing the Hanafi rule of shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional killing). The principle is: if an act is shibh 'amd with respect to killing the soul (i.e., it causes death but not with a deadly weapon), then for injuries less than death (e.g., breaking a tooth), the same act is treated as 'amd (intentional) and thus qisas is applicable. The reason: shibh 'amd in the soul was defined by the use of a tool that is not normally deadly, but for body parts, there is no such tool distinction; any act that would be shibh 'amd for the soul becomes 'amd for limbs. The proof is the hadith of al-Rubayyi': a slap (which could be shibh 'amd if it kills) broke a tooth, and the Prophet ordered qisas (retaliation) for the tooth. From this, we deduce that the slap could not be shibh 'amd for the tooth because that category does not apply to body parts. Thus, if a slap kills, it is shibh 'amd (no qisas for life, only diyah); if it breaks a tooth, it is 'amd (qisas for the tooth). This is a unique Hanafi position.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Quasi-intentional killing: the killer intended the act but not death, using a tool not normally deadly. In the soul, it requires diyah, not qisas.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind: taking life for life or injury for injury, when conditions are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person slaps another (which would be shibh 'amd if it causes death) and instead breaks a tooth, what is the ruling?
A) No qisas because it is shibh 'amd
B) Qisas is wajib for the tooth because it is treated as 'amd
C) Only diyah is wajib
D) No liability because the act was not intentional

Key Takeaway
Shibh 'amd in the soul is 'amd for all other body parts; thus qisas applies to injuries below the soul even when the same act would not warrant qisas for death.

Shibh al-Amd: Exclusively for Killing, Not Limbs
Ruling with proof: shibh al-amd limited to life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school restricts shibh al-amd to homicide and identify the hadith evidence that supports treating limb injuries as full amd.

Core Idea
Shibh al-amd only applies to killing; for limbs, any intentional strike is full amd.

This lesson clarifies a crucial Hanafi distinction: the category of quasi-intentional (shibh al-amd) exists only for homicide, not for injuries short of death. The key reasoning is that harming limbs can be done with any instrument, so intent is direct and unambiguous. The famous hadith of al-Rubayyi' provides the proof, where a slap breaking a tooth was ruled qisas—something that would not happen if the slap had killed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
Read the matn statement: 'And quasi-intentional regarding life is intentional regarding other than it.' This is the central rule.
The rule is a binary distinction: one category for killing, another for everything else.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (illah)
The commentator explains that causing harm to limbs is not restricted to a specific instrument; any instrument can cause injury. Therefore, there is no room for a quasi-intent category—intent is always clear.
Contrast with killing: some instruments rarely kill, so intent to kill may be uncertain.
3
Examine the hadith of al-Rubayyi'
Read the hadith carefully: a slap broke a front tooth; the Prophet ordered qisas (retaliation) for the tooth.
Note that the slap was not intended to kill, but the injury to the tooth was considered intentional.
4
Extract the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith proves that if the same slap had caused death, it would be shibh al-amd (no qisas). But for the tooth, it was amd (qisas). This establishes the distinction.
The ruling for the tooth shows that limbs are always amd; the killing scenario remains shibh al-amd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال رحمه الله: (وشبه العمد في النفس عمد فيما سواها) لأن إتلاف ما دون النفس لا يختصر بآلة دون آلة فلا يتصور فيه شبه العمد بخلاف النفس على ما بينا والذي يدلك على هذا ما روي عن أنس ابن مالك أن عمة الربيع لطمت جارية فكسرت ثنيتها فطلبوا إليهم العفو فأبوا والأرش فأبوا إلا القصاص واختصموا إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فأمر رسول الله بالقصاص فقال أنس بن النضر : أتكسر ثنية الربيع والذي بعثك بالحق نبياً لا تكسر ثنيتها فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : يا أنس كتاب الله القصاص فرضي القوم فعفوا فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن من عباد الله من لو أقسم على الله لأبره. ووجه دلالته على ما نحن فيه أننا علمنا أن اللطمة لو أتت على النفس لا توجب القصاص ورأيناها فيما دون النفس قد أوجبته بحكمه عليه الصلاة والسلام فثبت بذلك أن ما كان من النفس شبه عمد فهو عمد فيما دونها ولا يتصور أن يكون شبه عمد والله أعلم.
And he (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And quasi-intentional (shibh al-amd) regarding life (nafs) is intentional (amd) regarding other than it (limbs).' Because destruction of what is less than life is not restricted to a specific instrument without an instrument, so quasi-intentional cannot be conceived in it, contrary to life as we have explained. And what indicates this to you is what is narrated from Anas ibn Malik that the aunt of al-Rubayyi' slapped a slave girl and broke her front tooth. They asked them for forgiveness but they refused, and they offered compensation but they refused except qisas. They brought the case to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and the Messenger of Allah ordered qisas. Anas ibn al-Nadr said: 'Will you break the front tooth of al-Rubayyi'? By Him Who sent you with the truth as a Prophet, her front tooth shall not be broken.' The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'O Anas, the Book of Allah prescribes qisas.' So the people were satisfied and forgave. Then the Messenger of Allah said: 'Indeed among the servants of Allah are those who, if they swear by Allah, Allah fulfills it.' The aspect of its indication to our subject is that we know that if the slap had resulted in death, it would not necessitate qisas, but we saw that regarding what is less than life, it necessitated it by his ruling, peace be upon him. So it is established thereby that what is quasi-intentional regarding life is intentional regarding what is less than it, and quasi-intentional cannot be conceived. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It states a key Hanafi principle: the category of shibh al-amd (quasi-intentional) applies only to killing (nafs), not to injuries short of death (ma siwa al-nafs). The reason given is that causing harm to limbs can be done with various instruments, so there is no distinction between direct and indirect intent; any intentional harmful act to a limb is full amd. The author supports this with the hadith of al-Rubayyi', where a slap broke a tooth, and the Prophet ordered qisas. The Hanafi jurists argue that if the same slap had caused death, it would not require qisas (because it would be shibh al-amd), but for the tooth, it required qisas, proving that the category of shibh al-amd does not exist for limbs. This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school and is relied upon (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-amd): Quasi-intentional killing: the killer intended to strike but not to kill, using an instrument that usually does not kill.
النفس (al-nafs): Life; killing a person, as opposed to causing injury short of death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It requires compensation but not qisas.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies shibh al-amd exclusively for homicide; any intentional harmful act to a limb is full amd, supported by the hadith of al-Rubayyi'.

Intra-Hanafi Disputes on Indirect Killing: Sword, Fire, and Drowning
Intra-Hanafi disputes on three indirect killing scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'amd (intentional) and khata' al-'amd (quasi-intentional) in three cases of indirect killing and state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn for each scenario.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires near-certain death for qisas in indirect killing; the Sahibayn impose qisas if death is overwhelmingly likely.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents three classic scenarios of indirect killing: striking with a sword in its sheath, throwing into a fire, and drowning. The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa generally classifies these as khata' al-'amd (no qisas) if any survival chance exists, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat them as 'amd when death is virtually certain. Understanding this dispute is crucial for a mufti determining whether qisas applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and key variables
Read each case (sword, fire, drowning and note the factors: sheath strength, water depth, swimming ability, time between act and death).
These variables determine the likelihood of survival.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and reasoning
For each case, note whether Abu Hanifa holds khata' al-amd or 'amd, and the basis: any doubt about causation prevents qisas.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and reasoning
For each case, note their counter-position: if death is virtually certain, it is 'amd and qisas is obligatory.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
For each scenario, identify whether the difference leads to qisas (Sahibayn) or diyah on 'aqilah (Abu Hanifa).
5
Note the mufta bihi position where indicated
The text mentions fatwa on Abu Hanifa's view in the sword case; for drowning, later texts show variation.
A mufti must weigh the specific circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي العيون : ضرب رجلاً بسيف في غمده فخرق السيف الغمد وقتله قال أبو حنيفة : لا قصاص عليه. وقال محمد: إن كان الغمد لو ضرب به وحده قتل قتل به. وفي الكبرى والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة . قال محمد في الجامع الصغير : إذا حمى التنور فألقى فيها إنساناً أو ألقاه فيما لا يستطيع الخروج منه فأحرقته النار يجب القصاص فوضع المسألة . يصير إلى أن الإحماء يكفي، وإن لم يكن فيه نار قال البقالي في فتاواه : هو الصحيح. وفي البقالي : إذا ألقاه في النار ثم أخرجه وبه رمق فبقي أياماً مريضاً ذلك حتى مات قتل به وإن كان يجيء ويذهب. وفي الخانية : فمكث أياماً لم يزل صاحب فراش، وإن كان يجيء ويذهب فلا . وفي الجامع الصغير أيضاً : وذكر شيخ الإسلام في شرح ديات الأصل إن غرق إنساناً بالماء إن كان الماء قليلاً لا يقبل منه غالباً ويرجى منه النجاة في الغالب فمات فهو خطأ العمد عندهم جميعاً، فأما إذا كان الماء عظيماً إن كان بحيث يمكنه النجاة منه بالسباحة بأن كان غير مشدود ولا مثقل وهو يحسن السباحة فمات فإنه يكون خطأ العمد، وإن كان بحيث لا يمكنه النجاة فعلى قول أبي حنيفة هو خطأ العمد فلا قصاص، وعلى قولهما هو عمد محض ويجب القصاص. وفي الخانية : ولو ألقاه في الماء فغرق من ساعته لا قصاص في قول أبي حنيفة، وفي قول صاحبيه يجب القصاص. وفي المنتقى عن أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة : رمى رجلاً من سفينة في بحر أو في دجلة أو غرق كما وقع فعلى عاقلته الدية، وإن كان حين ألقاه ساعة سبح ثم غرق فلا دية فيه ولو ألقاه من سطح أو جبل أو ألقاه في بئر فعلى قول أبي حنيفة هذا خطأ العمد أما على قولهما إن كان موضعاً النجاة غالباً فهو خطأ، وإن كان لا ترجى منه النجاة غالباً فهو عمد محض يجب القصاص به.
And in al-'Uyun: A man struck another with a sword in its sheath, so the sword pierced the sheath and killed him. Abu Hanifa said: No qisas upon him. Muhammad said: If the sheath alone, when struck with, would kill, then he is killed for it. In al-Kubra and the fatwa is on the statement of Abu Hanifa. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If he heated an oven and threw a person into it, or threw him into something from which he cannot exit, and the fire burned him, qisas is obligatory. He placed the issue: It leads to that heating is sufficient, even if there was no fire in it. Al-Baqqali said in his Fatawa: That is correct. And in al-Baqqali: If he threw him into the fire then removed him while he still had life, and he remained days sick from that until he died, he is killed for him, even if he came and went. And in al-Khaniyyah: He stayed days never leaving his bed, but if he came and went, then no. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir also: And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned in Sharh Diyat al-Asl that if he drowned a person in water, if the water is little from which he is not often saved and rescue is expected in most cases, then he dies, it is khata' al-amd according to all of them. As for if the water is great, if it is such that he can save himself by swimming, being not bound nor weighed down, and he knows how to swim, then he dies, it is khata' al-amd. And if it is such that he cannot be saved, then according to Abu Hanifa it is khata' al-amd, so no qisas; and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is pure amd and qisas is obligatory. And in al-Khaniyyah: If he threw him into water and he drowned instantly, no qisas according to Abu Hanifa; according to the two companions, qisas is obligatory. And in al-Muntaqa from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa: He threw a man from a ship into the sea or into the Tigris or he drowned as it happened, then the diyah is upon his 'aqilah. And if when he threw him he swam for an hour then drowned, no diyah in it. And if he threw him from a roof or a mountain or threw him into a well, then according to Abu Hanifa this is khata' al-amd; as for according to the two, if it is a place where rescue is often possible, it is khata'; and if rescue is rarely hoped for, it is pure amd, qisas is obligatory for it.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents a series of intra-Hanafi disputes regarding indirect killing (qatl bi-sabab). The core issue is whether the act constitutes 'amd (intentional) or khata' al-'amd (quasi-intentional, i.e., shibh al-amd). Abu Hanifa generally holds that if the victim had any chance of survival, it is khata' al-'amd, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider overwhelming likelihood of death as 'amd. The cases include: hitting with a sword in its sheath (no qisas for Abu Hanifa if the sheath alone wouldn't kill); throwing into a fire (qisas if heating is sufficient, and if the victim dies after a period, qisas only if bedridden continuously); drowning (detailed distinctions based on water size and swimming ability). In practice, the fatwa often follows Abu Hanifa, as in the sword case, but for drowning, the Sahibayn's view may be closer to the mufta bihi in some contexts. The passage shows the Hanafi method of analyzing causation and intent.

Key Terms
خطأ العمد (khata' al-amd): Quasi-intentional killing: the killer's act was deliberate but the death was not intended and the means were not normally lethal.
الإحماء (al-ihma'): Heating (the oven) as a preparatory act that makes the killing intentional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Abu Hanifa's ruling on a person who throws another into deep water from which the victim cannot escape and he drowns?
A) It is 'amd mahd (pure intentional) and qisas is obligatory.
B) It is khata' al-'amd and no qisas, but diyah on 'aqilah.
C) It is purely accidental (khata') with no penalty.
D) It requires both qisas and diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, indirect killing is classified as either khata' al-amd or 'amd based on the likelihood of death, with Abu Hanifa requiring near-certainty for qisas and the Sahibayn requiring only overwhelming probability.

Poisoning and Homicide Classification: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on poisoning homicide with intra-Hanafi dispute on 'amd/khata classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of poisoning homicide in Hanafi fiqh and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over classification as 'amd or khata' al-'amd.

Core Idea
Forced poisoning is always khata' al-'amd for Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn distinguish by lethal dose.

Homicide by poison presents a unique challenge in Hanafi law because the killing is indirect. The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq outlines three scenarios and then examines whether the act is intentional murder ('amd) requiring qisas or semi-intentional (khata' al-'amd) requiring only diyah. The central dispute is between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples over how to classify forced poisoning. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi categories of homicide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios of poisoning homicide
The passage lists three ways poison can be administered: (1) forcing it down the throat (ījār), (2) compelling the victim to drink under duress, (3) handing the poison and the victim drinks voluntarily. List each and note the initial ruling for forced and compelled cases.
The third scenario (voluntary drinking) results in no liability.
2
Determine the ruling for forced/compelled poisoning according to the text
The passage states: 'no qisas, diyah on the 'aqlah'. This is the general ruling applied by Abu Hanifa. Note that the text says the issue is mentioned 'without dispute' in al-Dhakhira, meaning earlier Hanafi sources gave no further detail.
This ruling is consistent with khata' al-'amd.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's position and its rationale
Abu Hanifa holds that any forced poisoning is khata' al-'amd, regardless of dose. His reasoning: the killing occurs through an indirect cause (the poison works internally, not by a direct blow), and the act does not exit the category of semi-intentional homicide either in reality or in legal consideration.
He treats all forced poisonings uniformly.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's position and the disagreement
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, according to some later scholars, differentiate: if the dose is lethal (would normally kill), it is pure 'amd (intentional murder); if non-lethal, it is khata' al-'amd. The practical difference: qisas is possible only in the lethal-dose scenario under their view.
The passage also mentions a view that the Sahibayn agree with Abu Hanifa 'absolutely' (mutlaqan) – an unresolved nuance.
5
Apply the ruling to the knife suicide analogy
The passage ends with an analogous case: handing a knife to someone who then kills himself. The giver incurs no liability. This reinforces the principle that voluntary self-destruction severs the causal link.
Contrast this with forced poisoning where liability exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا سقا رجلاً سماً فمات من ذلك فهو على ثلاثة أوجه : إما أن يكون أوجره على كره أو أكرهه على شربه حتى شرب أو ناوله وشربه من غير أن يكرهه عليه فإن أوجره إيجاراً أو ناوله وأكزهه على شربه حتى شرب فلا قصاص وعلى عاقلته الدية. وفي الذخيرة ذكر المسألة في الأصل من غير خلاف ولم يفصل بين ما إذا كان مقدراً يقتل مثله غالباً أو لا يقتل، وهذا الجواب لا يشكل على قول أبي حنيفة وذلك لأن القتل حصل يحال لا يخرج لا من حيث الحقيقة ولا من حيث الاعتبار فكان خطأ العمد على مذهبه وأما على قول أبي يوسف ومحمد فمن مشايخنا من قال الجواب عندهما على التفصيل إن كان ما أوجره من السم مقدار ما يقتل مثله غالباً كان عمداً محضاً، وإن كان قدراً لا يقتل مثله غالباً فإنه يكون خطأ العمد ومن البحر الرائق ج ٩/ م ٢ مطلقاً وإذا تناوله فشرب من غير أن أكره لم يكن عليه قصاص ولا دية سواء علم الشارب بكونه سماً أو لم يعلم. وفي الخانية : لا قصاص عليه ولا دية لأنه شرب باختياره إلا أن الدافع خدعه فلا يجب عليه إلا التعزيز والاستغفار. ومن دفع سكيناً إلى رجل فقتل به نفسه لم يكن على الدافع شيء.
And if he gives a man poison and he dies from it, it is of three types: either he forced it down his throat against his will, or he compelled him to drink it until he drank, or he handed it to him and he drank without being forced. If he forced it down or handed it and compelled him to drink until he drank, then no qisas and the diyah is on his 'aqlah. In al-Dhakhira, the issue is mentioned in the original without dispute, and it does not differentiate between whether it is a quantity that would normally kill or not. This answer is not problematic for Abu Hanifa's view, because the killing occurred in a manner that does not exit from [the category] neither in reality nor in consideration, so it is khata' al-'amd according to his madhhab. As for the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, some of our scholars said the answer according to them is detailed: if the forced poison was in a quantity that would normally kill, then it is pure 'amd; if it is a quantity that would not normally kill, then it is khata' al-'amd. Absolutely. And if he took it and drank without being forced, there is no qisas and no diyah, whether the drinker knew it was poison or not. In al-Khaniyya: There is no qisas upon him and no diyah because he drank by his own choice, unless the giver deceived him, then only ta'zir and istighfar are required. And if someone hands a knife to a man and he kills himself with it, there is nothing on the giver.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage details the rulings on homicide by poison, a technically complex area. Three scenarios: (1) force-feeding or compelling to drink — no qisas (because the act is not direct like a blow), diyah on the 'aqlah (paternal relatives). (2) Handing poison and the victim drinks voluntarily — no liability at all, as the victim is the agent of his own death. (3) Deception (the giver tricks the victim) — only ta'zir. The text then discusses an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal classification of the act. Abu Hanifa consistently treats it as 'khata' al-'amd' (semi-intentional homicide: intended act but not intending death), thus diyah on the 'aqlah, not qisas. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require distinction based on the dose: if the poison dose is lethal, it is pure 'amd (intentional murder) warranting qisas; if not lethal, it is khata' al-'amd. The passage notes that some later scholars say the Sahibayn universally consider it khata' al-'amd. The final part gives an analogous ruling: giving a knife to someone who then commits suicide incurs no liability.

Key Terms
خطأ العمد (khata' al-'amd): Semi-intentional homicide: the act is intentional (e.g., administering poison) but the intent to kill is not established, resulting in diyah on the 'aqlah.
إيجار (ījār): Forcing something into the throat of another, typically against their will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone forces poison down another's throat and the victim dies, under Abu Hanifa's view?
A) Qisas is obligatory.
B) Diyah on the 'aqlah, no qisas.
C) No liability because the victim died from poison.
D) The perpetrator must pay a fine and serve prison.

Key Takeaway
Forced poisoning is classified as khata' al-'amd by Abu Hanifa (diyah only), while the Sahibayn distinguish by lethal dose (qisas possible if lethal); the later fatwa often follows Abu Hanifa.

Liability for Death by Imprisonment and Starvation: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for indirect killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for death caused by imprisonment and starvation, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Imprisonment causing death incurs no diyah under Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn impose it.

When a person causes another's death indirectly, such as by imprisonment or starvation, is he liable for diyah? The Hanafi school has a well-known internal dispute. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the core positions and the fatwa relied upon. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi law in cases of indirect causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the causative acts
The passage covers three scenarios: imprisonment/starvation, confinement until death, and burial alive. Identify the act in each case and the consequence (death).
2
State Abu Hanifa’s ruling
For imprisonment and starvation, Abu Hanifa holds that there is no liability (no diyah, no qisas). The death is attributed to external factors, not the actor’s direct act.
The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa’s view.
3
State the Sahibayn’s ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that diyah is obligatory on the 'aqlah. They see the act as a direct cause of death.
Muhammad sometimes adds ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
4
Analyze the burial scenario
For throwing a living person into a grave, some sources say qisas (direct intentional killing), but others say diyah. The fatwa in al-Zahiriya and al-Kubra is that diyah is on the 'aqlah, not qisas.
This shows nuance: burial alive is closer to direct killing, but the fatwa still follows Abu Hanifa’s principle of indirect causation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text repeatedly states that the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa’s view: no liability for imprisonment/starvation; for burial, diyah on the 'aqlah.
This is the operative ruling for Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادخل نائماً أو مغمى عليه أو صبياً في بيته فسقط عليه البيت ضمن في الصبي والمعتوه دون النائم، إن أدخل إنساناً في بيت حتى مات جوعاً أو عطشاً لا يضمن شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تجب الدية. وفي الكبرى : إذا طين على آخر بيتاً حتى مات جوعاً أو عطشاً لم يضمن شيئاً في قول أبي حنيفة. وقالا : عليه الدية. وفي الخانية قال محمد: يعاقب الرجل وعلى عاقلته الدية . وفي الظهيرية : ولو أن رجلاً أخذ رجلاً فقيده وحبسه حتى مات جوعاً قال محمد أوجعه عقوبة والدية على عاقلته. والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة أنه لا شيء عليه. وفي المنتقى سئل محمد عن رجل ألقى رجلاً حياً في قبر ومات قال : فيه دية. وفي الذخيرة : يقاد فيه لأنه قتله عمداً. وفي الكبرى : ولو ألقاه حياً في قبر يقتل به لأنه قتله عمداً وهذا قول محمد والفتوى أنه على عاقلته الدية. وفي الظهيرية والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة.
He entered a sleeping person, or one unconscious, or a child into his house and the house collapsed on them: he is liable for the child and the insane person, but not for the sleeping person. If he took a person into a house and he died of hunger or thirst, he is not liable for anything according to Abu Hanifa, but according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] diyah is obligatory. In al-Kubra: If he plastered [a wall] on another in a house until he died of hunger or thirst, he is not liable for anything according to Abu Hanifa. They two said: diyah is upon him. In al-Khaniyya, Muhammad said: The man is punished and diyah is on his 'aqlah. In al-Zahiriya: And if a man took a man, tied him up, and imprisoned him until he died of hunger, Muhammad said: He is punished and diyah is on his 'aqlah. The fatwa is according to Abu Hanifa that there is nothing upon him. In al-Muntaqa, Muhammad was asked about a man who threw a living man into a grave and he died; he said: There is diyah. In al-Dhakhira: Qisas is applied because he intentionally killed him. In al-Kubra: And if he threw him alive into a grave, he is killed for it because he intentionally killed him, and this is the view of Muhammad, but the fatwa is that diyah is on his 'aqlah. In al-Zahiriya: The fatwa is according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents several scenarios of causing death through indirect means (imprisonment, starvation, burial) and the intra-Hanafi dispute. The common thread: Abu Hanifa generally denies liability (no diyah, no qisas) because the death is caused by an external factor (hunger, thirst, structure collapse) and the act is not a direct killing. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) often impose diyah, considering the act as a cause of death. Muhammad sometimes adds punishment (ta'zir). For burial alive, there is a strong difference: some sources (al-Dhakhira) say qisas because it is direct intentional killing, while others (al-Muntaqa, al-Kubra) say diyah only. The fatwa in al-Zahiriya and elsewhere is consistently given on Abu Hanifa's view (no liability or diyah on 'aqlah, not qisas). Note the nuance: if a house collapses on a sleeping adult, no liability because the victim's own act of entering voluntarily negates the tort; for a child or insane person, liability because they cannot consent.

Key Terms
ضمن (ḍamina): To be liable for compensation (diyah or damages).
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The paternal relatives of the killer who pay diyah in cases of semi-intentional or accidental homicide.
قتل عمد (qatl ʿamd): Intentional killing, which normally incurs qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa stated in al-Zahiriya and al-Kubra, what is the ruling on a man who imprisons another until he dies of hunger?
A) Qisas is obligatory.
B) Diyah is obligatory on the 'aqlah.
C) There is no liability (no diyah, no qisas).
D) The man must pay diyah from his own wealth.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, causing death by imprisonment or starvation generally incurs no diyah per Abu Hanifa, whose view is the basis for fatwa.

Conflicting Confessions in Homicide
Rulings on conflicting confessions in homicide.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for when a confession of homicide is averted by a claim of mistaken identity and when retractions are rejected, and apply the rule of uncertainty about cause of death to determine half diyah.

Core Idea
A confession of murder can be averted by a credible claim of mistake, but a retraction after the confession is established is not accepted.

This lesson examines several scenarios from al-Mujarrad and al-Muntaqa where a person confesses to killing, then either claims mistaken identity, retracts by saying the act was done by another, or states he was a minor at the time. The Hanafi school sets clear rules about which claims are accepted and which are rejected. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying qisas and diyah correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the confession and the subsequent claim
First, note that the person admits to killing with a specific weapon. Then he makes an additional statement that conflicts with the admission (e.g., 'I intended someone else' or 'it wasn't me' or 'I was a minor').
Each scenario is treated differently based on the nature of the claim.
2
Apply the rule for mistaken identity
If the killer says he intended someone else and struck the actual victim by mistake, qisas is averted (duri'a anhu al-qatl). This is based on a narration from Abu Hanifa by Hasan ibn Ziyad.
The claim of mistake is credible if the killer could have genuinely erred.
3
Apply the rule for retraction by attributing to another
If the confessor says 'we killed him' then says 'someone else killed him', his retraction is not believed, and he is executed (qisas). The confession held.
Once a confession is established, shifting blame to another is not accepted.
4
Apply the rule for claiming minority at the time
If the killer says he was a minor on the day of the killing, this retraction is also rejected, and qisas is carried out.
Minority must be proven by evidence; mere claim after confession is insufficient.
5
Apply the rule for uncertainty about cause of death
If the killer admits striking but says he does not know if the victim died from the blow, while the guardian insists it did, the killer's statement is taken and he pays half diyah (not qisas).
Uncertainty about causation reduces liability from qisas to diyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجرد روى الحسن بن زياد عن أبي حنيفة : فلان قتله بحديدة أو قال بالسيف ثم قال إنما أردت غيره فأصابته دريء عنه القتل. وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل قتلنا فلاناً بأسيافنا متعمدين ثم قال كان غيري لم يصدق وقتل به، ولو قال قتلت فلاناً متعمداً بحديدة فلما أخذ بذلك قال كنت يومئذ غلاماً لم يصدق وقتل به، ولو قال ضربت فلاناً بالسيف متعمداً ثم قال لا أدري مات منها أم لا ولكنه مات وقال الولي مات من ضربتك فالقول قول القاتل وعليه نصف الدية وفي المنتقى : إذا قطع حلقوم الرجل وبقي شيء قليل من الحلقوم وفيه الروح فقتله رجل آخر فلا قود عليه لأن هذا ميت، ولو مات ابنه بعد ذلك وهو على تلك الحالة ورثه ابنه ولم يرث هو من ابنه. وفي الظهيرية : رجل نائم وهو صحيح فذبحه إنسان وقال ذبحته وهو ميت فإنه يقتل به قياساً، وفي الاستحسان تجب الدية. ولو شق بطن رجل وخرج أمعاءه كلها وسقطت على الأرض إلا أنه صحيح بعد فقتله رجل فلا قود عليه. وفي الخانية: رجل عدا على رجل فشق بطنه وأخرج أمعاءه ثم ضرب رجل عنقه بالسيف عمداً فالقاتل هو الذي ضرب العنق عمداً، وإن كان خطأ تجب الدية، وعلى الذي شق البطن ثلث الدية، وإن كان نفذ إلى الجانب الآخر يجب ثلثاً الدية لأنهما حاشيتان في كل منهما ثلث الدية. هذا إذا كان مما يعيش بعد الشق يوماً أو بعض يوم، فإن كان الشق بحال لا يتوهم معه وجود الحياة ولم يبق معه إلا اضطراب الموت فالقاتل هو الذي شق البطن فيقتص في العمد وتجب الدية في الخطأ. ولو قتل رجلاً وهو في معي
And in al-Mujarrad, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa: So-and-so killed him with an iron [tool] or said with a sword, then said 'I only intended someone else and it struck him,' he is spared from qisas. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man says 'We intentionally killed so-and-so with our swords' and then said 'it was someone else,' he is not believed and is executed. And if he said 'I intentionally killed so-and-so with an iron [tool]' and when he was taken for that said 'I was a boy that day,' he is not believed and is executed. And if he said 'I struck so-and-so intentionally with a sword' and then said 'I don't know if he died from it or not, but he died,' and the guardian said 'He died from your strike,' the statement of the killer is accepted and he pays half the diyah. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man cuts the throat of another and a small part of the throat remains and in it is spirit, and another man kills him, then there is no qisas upon him because this [first] is dead. And if his son dies after that while in that state, his son inherits from him and he does not inherit from his son. And in al-Zahiriya: A man is sleeping and is healthy, and a person slits his throat and says 'I slit his throat while he was dead,' he is executed for it by qiyas, but by istihsan diyah is obligatory. And if a man slits the belly of another and all his intestines come out and fall on the ground, but he is still healthy, then another man kills him, no qisas upon him. And in al-Khaniyya: A man attacks another and slits his belly and removes his intestines, then another man strikes his neck with a sword intentionally: the killer is the one who struck the neck intentionally; if it was by mistake, diyah is obligatory, and on the one who slit the belly one-third of the diyah; if he penetrated to the other side, two-thirds of the diyah are obligatory because they are two sides, each containing one-third diyah. This applies if he could live after the slit for a day or part of a day. If the slit was such that survival is not conceivable and only the throes of death remain, then the killer is the one who slit the belly; in 'amd, qisas is taken; in khata, diyah is obligatory. And if he kills a man while he is in...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage compiles several distinct rulings on homicide from multiple sources. First, conflicting confessions: a person who admits killing but then claims mistaken identity may be spared if proven; however, retracting a confession after it is established (e.g., claiming minority or that another did it) is not accepted, and qisas is carried out. If the killer is uncertain whether his blow caused death, his word is taken over the guardian's, and he pays half diyah. Next, cutting the throat of a person already moribund (with only a bit of throat and life remaining) incurs no qisas because the victim is considered dead. The inheritance rule: if the son dies in that state, the father does not inherit from the son. For slitting the throat of a sleeping healthy person, qiyas demands qisas (since the victim was alive), but istihsan prefers diyah. Then, abdominal wounds: if a person's intestines are exposed but he is still alive, killing him later does not invoke qisas for the later killer. However, detailed rules from al-Khaniyya identify the killer based on whether the abdominal wound was survivable: if the victim could live a day or so, the neck-striker is the killer; if the belly wound is clearly fatal, the belly-splitter is the killer. The diyah amounts for non-fatal wounds are specified: if the knife penetrates to the other side, two-thirds diyah (one side each third). The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
دريء (duri’a): Spared or averted; here, the qisas is averted due to credible claim of mistake.
نصف الدية (nisf al-diyah): Half the blood money (diyah) — half of full diyah paid when causation is uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man says 'I struck so-and-so intentionally with a sword' but then says 'I don't know if he died from it,' and the guardian says he did, what is the ruling?
A) The killer is executed for qisas.
B) The killer pays full diyah.
C) The killer pays half diyah.
D) The killer is not liable because of doubt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a confession of homicide may be mitigated by a claim of mistaken identity or uncertainty about causation, but direct retractions of an established confession are rejected.

Throat-Cutting of a Moribund Person and Sleeping Person
Rulings on throat-cutting of moribund and sleeping persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why no qisas is imposed for killing a moribund person (whose life is minimal) and distinguish the inheritance effect; also state the ruling for slitting the throat of a sleeping healthy person, comparing qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
Killing a moribund person – one with only a bit of throat and life remaining – incurs no qisas because the victim is legally dead; but slitting the throat of a sleeping healthy person incurs diyah by istihsan, not qisas.

This lesson covers two distinct yet related rulings from al-Muntaqa and al-Zahiriya. The first deals with cutting the throat of a person already on the verge of death (moribund) – no qisas is due because the victim is considered dead. Additionally, inheritance rules are affected: a son dying in that state inherits from his father, but the father does not inherit from the son. The second ruling addresses slitting the throat of a sleeping healthy person: by qiyas (analogy) the killer would be executed, but istihsan (juristic preference) overrides and imposes diyah only.

Guided Path
1
Identify the victim's state in the first ruling
The victim has his throat cut but a bit remains, and he has some life (spirit) left. He is not completely dead but is considered moribund (nearly dead).
The text says 'وفيه الروح' (in it is spirit) meaning minimal life.
2
Apply the rule: no qisas for killing a moribund
Another person kills this moribund person. Since the victim is essentially already dying, the second killing does not cause death in a legal sense; hence no qisas.
The text: 'فلا قود عليه لأن هذا ميت' – no qisas because this (the first) is dead.
3
Apply the inheritance rule
If the son of the moribund person also dies in that same state (i.e., the moribund father is still alive when the son dies), the son inherits from the father, but the father does not inherit from the son. The father's moribund state blocks his inheritance.
The text: 'ولو مات ابنه بعد ذلك وهو على تلك الحالة ورثه ابنه ولم يرث هو من ابنه'.
4
Analyze the second ruling: sleeping healthy person
A healthy sleeping person has his throat slit. The killer claims the person was already dead. By qiyas, since the victim was alive, the killer would be executed for qisas. But istihsan (preferring a more equitable outcome) rules that only diyah is obligatory.
The text: 'يقتل به قياساً، وفي الاستحسان تجب الدية'.
5
Understand the role of istihsan
Qiyas is set aside in favor of istihsan due to the doubt created by the killer's claim. Istihsan avoids qisas when there is uncertainty about the victim's state at the time of the act.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi principle that allows departure from strict analogy to achieve justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجرد روى الحسن بن زياد عن أبي حنيفة : فلان قتله بحديدة أو قال بالسيف ثم قال إنما أردت غيره فأصابته دريء عنه القتل. وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل قتلنا فلاناً بأسيافنا متعمدين ثم قال كان غيري لم يصدق وقتل به، ولو قال قتلت فلاناً متعمداً بحديدة فلما أخذ بذلك قال كنت يومئذ غلاماً لم يصدق وقتل به، ولو قال ضربت فلاناً بالسيف متعمداً ثم قال لا أدري مات منها أم لا ولكنه مات وقال الولي مات من ضربتك فالقول قول القاتل وعليه نصف الدية وفي المنتقى : إذا قطع حلقوم الرجل وبقي شيء قليل من الحلقوم وفيه الروح فقتله رجل آخر فلا قود عليه لأن هذا ميت، ولو مات ابنه بعد ذلك وهو على تلك الحالة ورثه ابنه ولم يرث هو من ابنه. وفي الظهيرية : رجل نائم وهو صحيح فذبحه إنسان وقال ذبحته وهو ميت فإنه يقتل به قياساً، وفي الاستحسان تجب الدية. ولو شق بطن رجل وخرج أمعاءه كلها وسقطت على الأرض إلا أنه صحيح بعد فقتله رجل فلا قود عليه. وفي الخانية: رجل عدا على رجل فشق بطنه وأخرج أمعاءه ثم ضرب رجل عنقه بالسيف عمداً فالقاتل هو الذي ضرب العنق عمداً، وإن كان خطأ تجب الدية، وعلى الذي شق البطن ثلث الدية، وإن كان نفذ إلى الجانب الآخر يجب ثلثاً الدية لأنهما حاشيتان في كل منهما ثلث الدية. هذا إذا كان مما يعيش بعد الشق يوماً أو بعض يوم، فإن كان الشق بحال لا يتوهم معه وجود الحياة ولم يبق معه إلا اضطراب الموت فالقاتل هو الذي شق البطن فيقتص في العمد وتجب الدية في الخطأ. ولو قتل رجلاً وهو في معي
And in al-Mujarrad, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa: So-and-so killed him with an iron [tool] or said with a sword, then said 'I only intended someone else and it struck him,' he is spared from qisas. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man says 'We intentionally killed so-and-so with our swords' and then said 'it was someone else,' he is not believed and is executed. And if he said 'I intentionally killed so-and-so with an iron [tool]' and when he was taken for that said 'I was a boy that day,' he is not believed and is executed. And if he said 'I struck so-and-so intentionally with a sword' and then said 'I don't know if he died from it or not, but he died,' and the guardian said 'He died from your strike,' the statement of the killer is accepted and he pays half the diyah. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man cuts the throat of another and a small part of the throat remains and in it is spirit, and another man kills him, then there is no qisas upon him because this [first] is dead. And if his son dies after that while in that state, his son inherits from him and he does not inherit from his son. And in al-Zahiriya: A man is sleeping and is healthy, and a person slits his throat and says 'I slit his throat while he was dead,' he is executed for it by qiyas, but by istihsan diyah is obligatory. And if a man slits the belly of another and all his intestines come out and fall on the ground, but he is still healthy, then another man kills him, no qisas upon him. And in al-Khaniyya: A man attacks another and slits his belly and removes his intestines, then another man strikes his neck with a sword intentionally: the killer is the one who struck the neck intentionally; if it was by mistake, diyah is obligatory, and on the one who slit the belly one-third of the diyah; if he penetrated to the other side, two-thirds of the diyah are obligatory because they are two sides, each containing one-third diyah. This applies if he could live after the slit for a day or part of a day. If the slit was such that survival is not conceivable and only the throes of death remain, then the killer is the one who slit the belly; in 'amd, qisas is taken; in khata, diyah is obligatory. And if he kills a man while he is in...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage compiles several distinct rulings on homicide from multiple sources. First, conflicting confessions: a person who admits killing but then claims mistaken identity may be spared if proven; however, retracting a confession after it is established (e.g., claiming minority or that another did it) is not accepted, and qisas is carried out. If the killer is uncertain whether his blow caused death, his word is taken over the guardian's, and he pays half diyah. Next, cutting the throat of a person already moribund (with only a bit of throat and life remaining) incurs no qisas because the victim is considered dead. The inheritance rule: if the son dies in that state, the father does not inherit from the son. For slitting the throat of a sleeping healthy person, qiyas demands qisas (since the victim was alive), but istihsan prefers diyah. Then, abdominal wounds: if a person's intestines are exposed but he is still alive, killing him later does not invoke qisas for the later killer. However, detailed rules from al-Khaniyya identify the killer based on whether the abdominal wound was survivable: if the victim could live a day or so, the neck-striker is the killer; if the belly wound is clearly fatal, the belly-splitter is the killer. The diyah amounts for non-fatal wounds are specified: if the knife penetrates to the other side, two-thirds diyah (one side each third). The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
ميت (mayyit): Dead; in this context, the moribund person is considered legally dead.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here applied to demand qisas for killing a sleeping person.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; overrides qiyas to impose diyah instead of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a person who slits the throat of a healthy sleeping person and claims he was already dead?
A) He is executed by qiyas, but istihsan imposes diyah.
B) He is executed by both qiyas and istihsan.
C) He is not liable because the victim was sleeping.
D) He pays half diyah by qiyas.

Key Takeaway
Legal death (moribund state) precludes qisas for subsequent killing, while istihsan may reduce qisas to diyah when there is doubt about the victim's state.

Abdominal Wounds and Determining the Killer
Rulings on abdominal wounds and identifying the cause of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the identity of the killer when multiple wounds are inflicted, based on whether the abdominal wound is survivable or fatal, and calculate the diyah for non-fatal abdominal wounds.

Core Idea
When a victim suffers both an abdominal wound and a neck wound, the killer is the one who inflicted the fatal wound: the neck-striker if the abdominal wound was survivable, the belly-splitter if it was clearly fatal.

This lesson examines detailed rules from al-Khaniyya on a scenario where a victim is attacked by two people: one slits his belly and removes his intestines, and another strikes his neck. The determination of who is the actual killer depends on whether the abdominal wound was survivable (the victim could have lived for a day or part of a day) or instantly fatal (only throes of death remain). In addition, the diyah for non-fatal wounds that penetrate to the other side is specified: two-thirds diyah, one-third for each side of the abdomen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two attackers and their actions
Attacker A slits the belly and removes the intestines. Attacker B strikes the neck with a sword. The victim dies.
We need to determine which act caused death.
2
Assess the survivability of the abdominal wound
If the belly wound is such that the victim could have lived for a day or part of a day (survivable), then the neck strike is the cause of death. If the belly wound is such that survival is impossible (only throes of death remain), then the belly wound is the cause of death.
The text: 'هذا إذا كان مما يعيش بعد الشق يوماً أو بعض يوم' vs 'فإن كان الشق بحال لا يتوهم معه وجود الحياة ولم يبق معه إلا اضطراب الموت'.
3
Determine the killer based on survivability
If survivable: the one who struck the neck is the intentional killer (if his act was 'amd, qisas; if khata, diyah). If fatal: the one who slit the belly is the killer (qisas for 'amd, diyah for khata).
The text: 'فالقاتل هو الذي ضرب العنق عمداً' / 'فالقاتل هو الذي شق البطن'.
4
Calculate diyah for non-fatal abdominal wounds
If the belly wound is not fatal and the attacker does not kill, he pays diyah according to the severity: if the wound penetrates to the other side, two-thirds diyah (one-third for each side); otherwise, one-third diyah.
The text: 'وعلى الذي شق البطن ثلث الدية، وإن كان نفذ إلى الجانب الآخر يجب ثلثا الدية لأنهما حاشيتان في كل منهما ثلث الدية'.

Source Text

اضطراب الموت (iḍṭirāb al-mawt): Throes of death; the final agonal state before death.

Checkpoint
According to al-Khaniyya, if a belly wound is survivable (victim could live a day) and then a neck strike kills him, what diyah does the belly-splitter pay if his wound did not penetrate to the other side?
A) Full diyah.
B) Half diyah.
C) One-third diyah.
D) Two-thirds diyah.

Key Takeaway
The identity of the killer in a multi-wound homicide depends on which injury was fatal; survivability of the abdominal wound is the deciding factor, and non-fatal wound diyah is one-third or two-thirds based on penetration.

İntra-Hanafi Disputes on Indirect Homicide
Multiple rulings on indirect killing with differing Hanafi opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on key cases of indirect homicide and identify the mufta bihi according to the later Hanafi authorities.

Core Idea
Indirect causation does not automatically impose liability—Abu Hanifa's view of no liability is the relied-upon position in the school.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents multiple scenarios of indirect killing—from poisoning to imprisonment to throwing into a grave. The Hanafi school internal disputes are highlighted, with Abu Hanifa often ruling no liability while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) impose diya. The fatwa generally follows Abu Hanifa. Understanding these nuances is crucial for distinguishing between direct kill (qisas) and indirect causation (diya or none).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and act of causation
For each case, determine what the person did: gave poison, handed a knife, imprisoned, threw into a grave, etc. Classify whether the act directly or indirectly caused death.
The key is the causal link: direct act (stabbing) vs. indirect (imprisonment).
2
Determine the level of intent (amd, shibh amd, khata)
Check if the actor intended to kill (amd), acted semi-intentionally (shibh amd), or it was a pure accident (khata). The text introduces 'khata al-amd' for semi-intentional.
Shibh amd applies when the act is intentional but death is not the intended outcome.
3
Extract the positions of each Hanafi authority
Record what Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad say for each scenario. Note where they agree or disagree.
Often Abu Hanifa says no liability (la shay'a alayh), while the Sahibayn impose diya.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The text explicitly states 'wal-fatwa ala qawl Abi Hanifa' multiple times. Understand that the later school gives preference to Abu Hanifa in these indirect causation cases.
The mufta bihi is not necessarily the majority view but the one deemed strongest for fatwa.
5
Apply the rule to new hypothetical scenarios
Use the principle: direct killing with intent leads to qisas; indirect causation often leads to no liability (Abu Hanifa) or diya on asabah (Sahibayn).
The student should be able to predict the ruling for similar indirect killing cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات باب ما يوجب القصاص وما لا يوجبه مشايخنا من قال بأنه على قولهم جميعاً يكون خطأ العمد، سواء كان مما يقتل مثله غالباً أو لا يقتل، وكان كمن أوجر رجلاً سقمونيا لا تحتمله النفوس فمات لا يكون عمداً محضاً. وإذا تناوله فشرب من غير أن أكره لم يكن عليه قصاص ولا دية سواء علم الشارب بكونه سماً أو لم يعلم. وفي الخانية : لا قصاص عليه ولا دية لأنه شرب باختياره إلا أن الدافع خدعه فلا يجب عليه إلا التعزيز والاستغفار. ومن دفع سكيناً إلى رجل فقتل به نفسه لم يكن على الدافع شيء. وفي فتاوى الخلاصة : ادخل نائماً أو مغمى عليه أو صبياً في بيته فسقط عليه البيت ضمن في الصبي والمعتوه دون النائم، إن أدخل إنساناً في بيت حتى مات جوعاً أو عطشاً لا يضمن شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تجب الدية. وفي الكبرى : إذا طين على آخر بيتاً حتى مات جوعاً أو عطشاً لم يضمن شيئاً في قول أبي حنيفة. وقالا : عليه الدية. وفي الخانية قال محمد: يعاقب الرجل وعلى عاقلته الدية . وفي الظهيرية : ولو أن رجلاً أخذ رجلاً فقيده وحبسه حتى مات جوعاً قال محمد أوجعه عقوبة والدية على عاقلته. والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة أنه لا شيء عليه. وفي المنتقى سئل محمد عن رجل ألقى رجلاً حياً في قبر ومات قال : فيه دية. وفي الذخيرة : يقاد فيه لأنه قتله عمداً. وفي الكبرى : ولو ألقاه حياً في قبر يقتل به لأنه قتله عمداً وهذا قول محمد والفتوى أنه على عاقلته الدية. وفي الظهيرية والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة. وفي المجرد روى الحسن بن زياد عن أبي حنيفة : فلان قتله بحديدة أو قال بالسيف ثم قال إنما أردت غيره فأصابته دريء عنه القتل. وفي المنتقى : إذا قال الرجل قتلنا فلاناً بأسيافنا متعمدين ثم قال كان غيري لم يصدق وقتل به، ولو قال قتلت فلاناً متعمداً بحديدة فلما أخذ بذلك قال كنت يومئذ غلاماً لم يصدق وقتل به، ولو قال ضربت فلاناً بالسيف متعمداً ثم قال لا أدري مات منها أم لا ولكنه مات وقال الولي مات من ضربتك فالقول قول القاتل وعليه نصف الدية وفي المنتقى : إذا قطع حلقوم الرجل وبقي شيء قليل من الحلقوم وفيه الروح فقتله رجل آخر فلا قود عليه لأن هذا ميت، ولو مات ابنه بعد ذلك وهو على تلك الحالة ورثه ابنه ولم يرث هو من ابنه. وفي الظهيرية : رجل نائم وهو صحيح فذبحه إنسان وقال ذبحته وهو ميت فإنه يقتل به قياساً، وفي الاستحسان تجب الدية. ولو شق بطن رجل وخرج أمعاءه كلها وسقطت على الأرض إلا أنه صحيح بعد فقتله رجل فلا قود عليه. وفي الخانية: رجل عدا على رجل فشق بطنه وأخرج أمعاءه ثم ضرب رجل عنقه بالسيف عمداً فالقاتل هو الذي ضرب العنق عمداً، وإن كان خطأ تجب الدية، وعلى الذي شق البطن ثلث الدية، وإن كان نفذ إلى الجانب الآخر يجب ثلثاً الدية لأنهما حاشيتان في كل منهما ثلث الدية. هذا إذا كان مما يعيش بعد الشق يوماً أو بعض يوم، فإن كان الشق بحال لا يتوهم معه وجود الحياة ولم يبق معه إلا اضطراب الموت فالقاتل هو الذي شق البطن فيقتص في العمد وتجب الدية في الخطأ. ولو قتل رجلاً وهو في النزع فقتل القاتل به وإن كان يعلم أنه لا يعيش وسيأتي شيء من هذا الجنس. وفي فصل متفرقات الأسبيجابي : إذا شهد الشهود أنه ضربه فلم يزل صاحب فراش حتى مات فإن كان عمداً فعليه القصاص. وفي الجناية : رجل جرح رجلاً جراحة وآخر جراحة عمداً ثم صالح المجروح أحدهما عن الجرح وما يحدث منه على ما ثم مات منهما جميعاً عليه نصف الدية لوليه.
Book of Crimes: Chapter on what necessitates qisas and what does not. Some of our scholars said: according to all of them it is shibh amd (semi-intentional homicide), whether it is something that usually kills or not, and it is like someone who gave a man a poisonous medicine that souls cannot bear and he died—it is not pure amd. And if he took it and drank without coercion, there is no qisas or diya upon him, whether the drinker knew it was poison or not. In al-Khaniyya: No qisas or diya upon him because he drank by his choice, unless the giver deceived him, then nothing is obligatory except ta'zir and istighfar. And whoever hands a knife to a man and he kills himself with it, nothing is upon the giver. In Fatawa al-Khulasa: If he brought a sleeping person, or one unconscious, or a child into his house and the house fell on them, he is liable for the child and the insane person, but not the sleeping one. If he brought a person into a house until he died of hunger or thirst, he is not liable for anything according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the diya is obligatory. In al-Kubra: If he plastered a house over another until he died of hunger or thirst, he is not liable for anything according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: the diya is upon him. In al-Khaniyya, Muhammad said: The man is punished and the diya is upon his asabah. In al-Dhahiriyya: If a man took another, shackled him, and imprisoned him until he died of hunger, Muhammad said: he is to be punished and the diya upon his asabah. The fatwa is according to Abu Hanifa's saying that nothing is upon him. In al-Muntaqa: Muhammad was asked about a man who threw a living man into a grave and he died; he said: there is diya for him. In al-Dhakhira: He is subjected to qisas because he killed him intentionally. In al-Kubra: If he threw him alive into a grave, he is killed for it because he killed him intentionally—this is the saying of Muhammad, and the fatwa is that the diya is upon his asabah. In al-Dhahiriyya: The fatwa is according to Abu Hanifa. In al-Mujarrad, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa: If someone killed him with an iron, or said with a sword, then said 'I only intended someone else and it struck him,' the killing is averted from him. In al-Muntaqa: If a man said 'We killed so-and-so with our swords intentionally,' then said 'It was someone else,' he is not believed and is killed for it. And if he said 'I killed so-and-so intentionally with an iron,' and when he was seized for it he said 'I was a boy on that day,' he is not believed and is killed for it. And if he said 'I struck so-and-so with a sword intentionally,' then said 'I don't know if he died from it or not, but he died, and the guardian said he died from your strike,' the statement is the killer's and he owes half the diya. In al-Muntaqa: If someone cut a man's windpipe leaving a little part of it in which there is life, then another man killed him, there is no qisas upon him because he is dead. And if his son dies after that in that state, his son inherits from him, and he does not inherit from his son. In al-Dhahiriyya: A man who is sleeping and healthy, and a person slaughtered him and said 'I slaughtered him while he was dead,' then he is killed for it by qiyas, but by istihsan the diya is obligatory. And if someone split a man's belly and pulled out all his intestines and they fell to the ground, but he was still healthy afterwards, then another man killed him, there is no qisas upon him. In al-Khaniyya: A man attacked another, split his belly and removed his intestines, then another man struck his neck with a sword intentionally—the killer is the one who struck the neck intentionally; if it was by mistake, the diya is obligatory, and upon the one who split the belly is one-third of the diya; and if the cut reached the other side, two-thirds of the diya are obligatory because they are two sides, each having one-third of the diya. That is if it is something that one can live after splitting for a day or part of a day; if the splitting was in a condition such that life cannot be imagined with it and nothing remains except the throes of death, then the killer is the one who split the belly—qisas is taken in intentional cases and diya is obligatory in mistakes. And if he killed a man while he was in the throes of death, the killer is killed for him even if he knew he would not live; something of this type will come later. In the chapter of Miscellaneous Rulings of al-Asbijah: If witnesses testify that he struck him and he remained bedridden until he died, if it was intentional, then qisas is upon him. In al-Jinayat: A man wounded another with a wound, and another wounded him with a wound intentionally, then the wounded man made sulh with one of them regarding the wound and what arises from it, and then he died, upon them both is half the diya for his guardian.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from multiple Hanafi sources on cases where death results from indirect actions. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute compilation. The author presents various scenarios: giving a lethal poisonous substance, handing a knife for suicide, imprisoning someone leading to death by hunger/thirst, throwing someone alive into a grave, mistaken intent in killing, killing someone already in the death throes, and multiple wounds. For each scenario, the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad are reported, often with the fatwa according to Abu Hanifa. The key legal principle is that for qisas to apply, the killing must be direct and intentional (amd). Indirect causation often results in diya or nothing, depending on the level of causation and intent. The passage illustrates the Hanafi methodology of considering both qiyas and istihsan, and the hierarchy of authorities (Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, Fatawa).

Key Terms
خطأ العمد (khata' al-amd): Semi-intentional homicide; act intentionally done but not intended to kill, akin to quasi-intentional.
دية (diya): Financial compensation for homicide or injury paid to the victim's heirs.
عاقلة (aqila): The male agnate relatives who pay diya on behalf of the killer in cases of inadvertent homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the text, what is the liability of someone who imprisons another until he dies of thirst?
A) Qisas
B) Diya upon the killer's asabah
C) No liability
D) Diya upon the killer's own wealth

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, indirect causing of death by imprisonment or starvation incurs no liability according to the relied-upon view of Abu Hanifa, contrary to the Sahibayn who require diya.

Liability in Multiple Cause Deaths: Categorizing Actions as Hader or Madmin
Ruling on diya with multiple agents including self‑action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi categorization of actions (hader vs. madmin) to determine diya liability when multiple causes contribute to a death, and identify the effect of intentional vs. erroneous acts.

Core Idea
When multiple causes cause death, liability is apportioned by classifying each cause as null (hader), penalizable (madmin), or partially valid.

This lesson explores a nuanced scenario from Hanafi fiqh: a death resulting from the victim's own action, the action of Zayd, a lion, and a snake. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq provides a ruling, and the commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the legal reasoning by categorizing actions based on their consequences in this world and the Hereafter. Understanding this categorization is crucial for accurately apportioning diya (blood money) in multi‑cause deaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the matn: death occurs from the victim's own action, Zayd, a lion, and a snake. The matn rules that Zayd pays half the diya.
Note the inconsistency: the commentary later suggests one‑third. We will focus on the reasoning.
2
Classify each action as hader or madmin
According to the commentary, actions are categorized: lion and snake are hadar (null) in both worlds; the victim's own action is hadar in this world but sinful in the Hereafter (so partially considered); Zayd's action is madmin (fully considered in both worlds).
Hader actions carry no worldly liability; madmin actions carry liability.
3
Count the categories and apportion liability
There are three categories: (1) absolutely hader (lion/snake, one category), (2) partially considered (self‑action), (3) fully madmin (Zayd). The share is divided among the categories that are not absolutely hader. Thus Zayd bears one‑third of the diya (though the matn says half).
The inconsistency may be due to a different counting method; focus on the principle.
4
Determine payment source based on intent
If Zayd's act was intentional, diya is from his own wealth; if erroneous, from his 'aqila (male relatives).
This is the standard rule for liability distribution.
5
Apply the general principle for participation in killing
The passage cites al‑Mabsut: participants whose actions are not guaranteed (e.g., wild animals, apostates, self‑injury, or imam's legal amputation) do not subject the killer to qisas. If participants are guaranteed (erroneous killer, child, insane), no qisas on either; if erroneous, one diya is due.
This expands the scope beyond the specific scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وإن مات بفعل نفسه وزيد وأسد وحبة ضمن زيد نصف الدية) لأن فعل الأسد والحية جنس واحد لكونه هدراً في الدنيا والآخرة وفعله بنفس جنس آخر لكونه هدراً في الدنيا معتبراً في الآخرة حتى يأثم به، وفعل زيد معتبر في الدنيا والآخرة فصارت ثلاثة أجناس : هدر مطلقاً، الثابت فعلاً واحداً فيجب على زيد ثلث الدية، ثم إن كان فعل زيد عمداً تجب عليه الدية في ماله وإلا فعلى العاقلة لما عرف في موضعه. وفي المبسوط وغيره : المشاركة في القتل لا يخلوا إما أن يشارك القاتل من لا يكون فعله مضموناً أو يشاركه من يكون فعله مضموناً، فإن شاركه من لا يكون فعله مضموناً كالسبع والبهيمة والحربي والمرتد أو جرح انسان نفسه ثم جرحه آخر أو قطع الإمام يد السارق في سرقة ثم قطع آخر يده أو جرحه ومات فلا قصاص على القاتل بالإجماع، وإن شاركه من يكون فعله مضموناً كالخاطىء والصبي والمجنون فلا قصاص على واحد منهما ولو كان مكان العمد خطأ تجب دية واحدة، ولو جرحه رجلان ومعتبر ومعتبر من وجه دون وجه وهو فعله بنفسه فيكون
The author said: 'And if he dies by his own action and Zayd's action and a lion and a snake, Zayd is liable for half the diya.' Because the action of the lion and the snake are one category, as they are null (hadar) in this world and the next. His own action is another category, as it is null in this world but considered in the next, so he sins by it. And Zayd's action is considered in both worlds. So there are three categories: absolutely null; established as one action; so Zayd is liable for one-third of the diya. Then if Zayd's action was intentional, the diya is due from his wealth; otherwise from the 'aqila, as known in its place. In al-Mabsut and others: Participation in killing is not free from either the participation of one whose action is not guaranteed (madmin) or one whose action is guaranteed. If the participant is one whose action is not guaranteed, such as a wild animal, beast, harbi, or apostate, or a person wounding himself then another wounds him, or the imam cuts a thief's hand for theft then another cuts his hand, or wounds him and he dies, there is no qisas on the killer by consensus. If the participant is one whose action is guaranteed, such as an erroneous killer, a child, or an insane person, there is no qisas on either of them; and if instead of intentional it was erroneous, one single diya is due. And if two men wound him, and [actions are] considered and considered from one aspect and not from another, and his own action is ...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on liability when multiple causes contribute to death. The matn states: if a person dies from the combination of his own action, the action of Zayd, a lion, and a snake, then Zayd is liable for half the diya. The commentator explains the reasoning by categorizing the actions. The lion and snake's actions are 'null' (hadar) in both worldly and religious terms—they are not liable. The victim's own action is null in this world (no worldly liability on the deceased) but is sinful in the Hereafter (it is considered). Zayd's action is fully considered in both worlds. Thus there are three categories: (1) absolutely null (lion and snake), (2) null in this world but considered in the Hereafter (self-action), (3) fully considered (Zayd's action). The text says this yields three types, and Zayd's share is one-third of the diya (though the matn originally said half—this may be an inconsistency or the commentary adjusts). Then it specifies that if Zayd's act was intentional, the diya is from his own wealth; if it was erroneous, from his 'aqila. The passage then cites al-Mabsut for a general rule on participation in killing: if the participant is someone whose action is not guaranteed (like a wild animal, apostate, etc.), there is no qisas on the killer by consensus. If the participant is someone whose action is guaranteed (like an erroneous killer, child, insane), there is no qisas on either participant; and if the killing was erroneous, only one diya is due. The passage ends with a statement about two men wounding and the complexity of considering actions, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Null; actions that carry no liability in this world (though may be sinful in the Hereafter).
مضمون (madmin): Guaranteed; actions that entail liability (diya or qisas).
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of relatives who pay diya for erroneous killings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, if a person dies from his own action, Zayd's action, a lion, and a snake, how much diya does Zayd pay?
A) Full diya
B) Half the diya
C) One-third of the diya
D) No diya

Key Takeaway
When multiple causes cause death, Hanafi law categorizes each cause as hadar (null), partially considered, or madmin (fully liable), and apportions diya according to the number of effective categories.

Killing with a Spade: Conditions for Qisas and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on qisas for spade killing, distinguishing undisputed and disputed cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when hitting with a spade's iron edge versus its back or wood triggers qisas, and explain the internal Hanafi disagreement on the latter.

Core Idea
Qisas for a spade blow depends on which part strikes: iron edge is clear, back or wood is debated among Hanafi imams.

In this passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator explains the conditions for qisas when a person is killed with a mar (spade). The ruling hinges on whether the blow was struck with the iron edge (حد الحديد) or with the back of the iron or the wooden part. While the iron edge case is undisputed, the other cases reveal a disagreement among the early Hanafi authorities (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn). Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying qisas in cases involving spade-like tools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tool and its parts
Read the definition of mar: a long wooden stick with an iron head (wide iron piece) and a wooden crosspiece for the foot, used for digging. The iron head has an edge (حد) and a back (ظهر), and there is also the wooden shaft (عود).
The Persian term is 'bil' (meaning shovel/spade).
2
Understand the undisputed case
If the iron edge strikes and causes death, qisas is wajib without any disagreement. This is because the iron edge is a lethal cutting instrument.
This ruling is unanimous among the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
3
Analyze the disputed case
If the blow is with the back of the iron or with the wood, the ruling is not like strangulation/drowning. Instead, it falls under a previous dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, qisas is due (since the tool is iron and potentially lethal), while according to Sahibayn, qisas is not due (by analogy to strangulation, which is not typically a qisas-worthy method).
The passage does not resolve the dispute; it refers back to an earlier discussion.
4
Recall the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Memorize the positions: Abu Hanifa (qisas wajib) vs. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (no qisas). The practical difference is significant for a judge or mufti.
The mufta bihi (operative view) is not stated in this passage; later Hanafi works may prefer Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Apply to a real scenario
Consider a case where a person kills with the flat side of a spade. Use the distinction to determine which ruling to apply, and note that fatwa may depend on local Hanafi tradition.
This highlights the need to identify the exact striking part in homicide investigations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتله بمر يقتص أن أصابه الحديد وإلا لا كالخنق والتغريق هذا إذا أصابه بحد الحديد من غير خلاف، وإن أصابه بظهرها أو بالعود لا كالخنق والتغريق فهو على الخلاف الذي ذكرناه في أول الباب. والمر عود في طرفها حديدة قال العيني: المر بفتح الميم وتشديد الراء وهو خشبة طويلة في رأسها حديدة عريضة من فوقها خشبة عريضة يضع الرجل رجله عليها ويحفر بها الأرض، وبالفارسية تسمى بيل.
And if he kills him with a mar, he is subject to qisas if the iron struck him, otherwise not, like strangulation and drowning. This is if he struck him by the edge of the iron without dispute. And if he struck him by its back or with the wood, it is not like strangulation and drowning, but rather it is subject to the dispute we mentioned at the beginning of the chapter. The mar is a stick at its end is an iron. Al-Ayni said: al-mar with fatha on the mim and tashdid on the ra is a long wooden stick at its head a wide iron, above it a wide wooden piece; the man puts his foot on it and digs the ground with it, and in Persian it is called 'bil'.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains a ruling about killing with a tool called 'mar' (a spade). The ruling distinguishes based on the part of the tool that strikes. If the killer strikes with the iron edge (حد الحديد), then qisas is due if the killing was intentional, with no disagreement. However, if the strike is with the back of the iron or the wooden part, then the ruling is not clear-cut; it is subject to a previous dispute mentioned at the beginning of the chapter: some scholars treat it like an iron strike, while others deem it like strangulation (which does not require qisas because it lacks the cutting or piercing nature of iron). The passage also provides a definition of the mar: it is a long wooden stick with a wide iron piece at the head and another wooden crosspiece for the foot, used for digging (like a spade), called 'bil' in Persian.

Key Terms
المَر (al-mar): A long wooden stick with an iron head and a wooden footrest, used for digging; a spade.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliatory punishment (life for life) for intentional killing or wounding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Qisas for killing with a spade is undisputed when the iron edge strikes; other strikes are subject to a Hanafi internal disagreement.
