Causation in Homicide: The Bedridden Victim Rule
Ruling with proof on causation in homicide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a wound causes bedriddenness and death, and identify factors that sever the causal link.

Core Idea
Qisas is due when the wound is the apparent cause of death and no intervening factor severs causation.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi criminal law: when an intentional wound leads to the victim becoming bedridden and eventually dying, the wounder is subject to qisas. The ruling hinges on establishing a direct causal chain (sabab) between the wound and death. We will explore the reasoning behind this attribution and the exceptions that break the chain.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the key elements: intentional wound, victim becomes bedridden (firash), death occurs. Then state that qisas is wajib on the wounder.
The condition of bedriddenness is crucial — it shows the wound was severe and directly contributed to death.
2
Trace the causal reasoning (talil)
The author argues: the wound is dhahir (manifest) for death, so death is attributed to it (yuhal al-mawt 'alayh). Understand that this is a presumption of causation, not absolute certainty.
This is similar to the principle 'al-asl idafat al-hadath ila aqrab al-asbab'.
3
Identify factors that sever causation (qati' al-sabab)
Two examples are given: cutting the neck (hazz al-raqabah) — an independent cause of death — and complete recovery from the wound. If either occurs, the wounder is not liable for the death.
Complete recovery shows the wound healed, so death must be from another cause.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical
Test the rule: If A wounds B, B becomes bedridden, but then C cuts B's neck and B dies, A is not liable for the death. Only C is liable.
This illustrates how a later independent act severs the earlier causal link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ومن جرح رجلاً عمداً فصار فراش حتى مات يقتص ) يعني إذا جرح إنسان آخر فصار المجروح صاحب فراش حتى مات فإنه يقتص من الجارح لأن الجرح ظاهر لموته فيحال الموت عليه ما لم يوجد ما يقطعه كحز الرقبة أو البرء منه مطلقاً.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever wounds a man intentionally and he becomes bedridden until he dies, qisas is due.) Meaning: if a person wounds another, and the wounded becomes a bedridden person until he dies, then qisas is exacted from the wounder because the wound is apparent as the cause of his death, so death is attributed to it as long as something that severs causation does not exist, such as cutting the neck or complete recovery from it.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage explains a rule about causation in homicide. When a person intentionally wounds someone and the victim subsequently becomes bedridden and dies, the wound is considered the legal cause of death. Qisas is due on the wounder. However, this attribution holds only if no intervening factor severs the causal chain. Examples of such factors are: the victim's neck being cut (haz al-raqabah, a clear independent cause) or the wound healing completely (which shows the death was from another cause). The reasoning (talil) is that the wound is dhahir (apparent) for the death, so the death is referred back to it (yuhal al-mawt 'alayh).

Key Terms
فراش (firash): Bedridden state; the victim is confined to bed due to the wound.
حز الرقبة (hazz al-raqabah): Cutting the neck (a clear independent cause of death).
يقتص (yuqtass): Qisas is exacted; retributive punishment, typically retaliation for homicide or wounding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following factors severs the causal link between the wound and death, absolving the wounder from qisas?
A) The victim becomes bedridden.
B) The wound is deliberate.
C) The victim's neck is cut by a third party before death.
D) The victim dies on the same day.

Key Takeaway
Qisas is only due for a wound that is the apparent cause of death, and is not applied if an independent intervening cause severs the causal chain.

Liability in Multi-Cause Death: The Hanafi Rule
Ruling on liability in multi-cause death with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that liability for diyah in multi-cause death is divided by the number of liable human perpetrators, ignoring animal and self-inflicted acts, to determine the share of each liable party.

Core Idea
Only human acts count for liability; animals and self-harm are hadar and ignored in dividing diyah.

This passage from Bahar al-Ra'iq tackles a classic scenario: a person dies from a combination of his own act, Zayd's act, a lion, and a snake. How much diyah does Zayd owe? The Hanafi answer hinges on categorizing acts as 'mu'tabar' (considered) or 'hadar' (worthless). Only mu'tabar acts from human agents are divided equally; self-harm and animal acts are hadar and do not affect the division. This principle, derived from al-Mabsut, governs many multi-cause fatality cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contributing factors
List all acts that contributed to the death: the victim's own act, Zayd's act, lion's act, snake's act.
2
Categorize each act
Classify each as mu'tabar (liable human act) or hadar (worthless). Lion and snake: hadar absolutely. Victim's own act: hadar in this world (no liability), but sinful in the Hereafter. Zayd's act: mu'tabar (fully considered).
The victim's act is not a human perpetrator, so it is treated as hadar for liability purposes.
3
Apply the principle of division
Only mu'tabar acts are divided. Since Zayd is the only mu'tabar actor, he bears the full diyah? But the presence of other causes (even hadar) reduces his share because the death is attributed to multiple causes. According to the text, the hadar causes are all counted as one 'worthless' cause, and the mu'tabar cause is one, so the diyah is split equally between the worthless and mu'tabar categories. Thus Zayd pays half (or one-third based on a different count).
The matn says half; the sharh calculates one-third. The relied-upon position is half per the matn.
4
Determine the nature of Zayd's act
If Zayd's act was intentional, the diyah is from his own wealth; if mistaken, it is paid by his 'aqilah.
This distinction is standard Hanafi law for murder vs. manslaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وإن مات بفعل نفسه وزيد وأسد وحية ضمن زيد نصف الدية ) لأن فعل الأسد والحية جنس واحد لكونه هدراً في الدنيا والآخرة وفعله بنفس جنس آخر لكونه هدراً في الدنيا معتبراً في الآخرة حتى يأثم به، وفعل زيد معتبر في الدنيا والآخرة فصارت ثلاثة أجناس : هدر مطلقاً، الثابت فعلاً واحداً فيجب على زيد ثلث الدية، ثم إن كان فعل زيد عمداً تجب عليه الدية في ماله وإلا فعلى العاقلة لما عرف في موضعه. وفي المبسوط وغيره : المشاركة في القتل لا يخلوا إما أن يشارك القاتل من لا يكون فعله مضموناً أو يشاركه من يكون فعله مضموناً، فإن شاركه من لا يكون فعله مضموناً كالسبع والبهيمة والحربي والمرتد أو جرح انسان نفسه ثم جرحه آخر أو قطع الإمام يد السارق في سرقة ثم قطع آخر يده أو جرحه ومات فلا قصاص على القاتل بالإجماع، وإن شاركه من يكون فعله مضموناً كالخاطىء والصبي والمجنون فلا قصاص على واحد منهما ولو كان مكان العمد خطأ تجب دية واحدة، ولو جرحه رجلان عامداً ثم مات أحد الجارحين ثم مات المجروح أو رمى رجلان إلى آخر فمات أحدهما ثم أصاب السهمان فمات من ذلك هل يجب القصاص على الحي؟ قال بعضهم: يجب لأن فعل كل واحد منهما موجب وقال بعضهم: لا يجب لأن فعل أحدهما إنما ينعقد موجباً بعد الإصابة فلا ينعقد أحدهما موجباً بانفراده رجلان قتلا رجلاً أحدهما بالسيف والآخر بالعصا يقضي بالدية على عاقله صاحب العصا والقصاص على صاحب السيف. وفي المبسوط: أصله أن النفس متى تلفت بجنايات ووجب المال فإنه ينظر؛ إن تلفت بجنايات بني آدم فالعبرة فيها بعدد الجاني ولا عبرة بعدد الجنايات في حق الضمان حتى لو جرح واحد عشر جراحات خطأ وجرحه آخر واحدة خطأ بالدية عليهما نصفان لأن فعل الإنسان في نفسه معتبر لأنه لا ينقلب عن حكمه في الدنيا وهو القصاص والدية أو إلاثم في الآخرة فاعتبر عدد الجاني لا عدد الجنايات لأن كل جناية تصلح أن تكون سبب الموت لو انفردت والعلة لا تترجح بالزيادة من جنسها فاعتبر الكل جناية واحدة، وإذا تلفت بجنايات البهائم وبجنايات بني آدم فلا عبرة بعدد الجنايات لأن فعل البهائم هدر أصلاً لأنه لا يناط به حكم ما فاعتبر جنایات البهائم كلها كجناية واحدة لأن حكم الكل واحد وهو الهدر، وهذا كرجل به جروح ودماميل قاتله فجرحه رجل آخر فمات من الكل يضمن الجارح نصف الدية وبرفع النصف ويسقط عنه اعتبار عدد الدماميل لأنها مهدرة ولو قطع رجل يده ولصاحبه حجر وعقره كلب فكسر رجله وافترسه سبع فعلى القاطع نصف الدية لأن النفس تلفت بجنايات أربع واحدة فصار كأنها تلفت بجنايتين إحداهما معتبرة والأخرى مهدرة. ولو قطع يده رجل وجرحه آخر وجرح هو أيضاً نفسه وافترسه سبع ضمن القاطع ربع الدية والجارح ربعها لأن النفس تلفت بجنايات أربعة ثنتان منها من بني آدم وهو معتبرتان، وواحدة من غير بني آدم وهي مهدرة فقد تلفت بجناية كل واحد من الأجنبين ربعه وقد سبق بيانه.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he dies by the act of himself, Zayd, a lion, and a snake, then Zayd is liable for half the diyah.) Because the act of the lion and the snake are one genus in being worthless (hadar) in this world and the Hereafter, and his own act is another genus in being worthless in this world but considered in the Hereafter such that he sins by it, and the act of Zayd is considered in this world and the Hereafter. So they became three genera: absolutely worthless, one type of act is established as a single act, and so Zayd is liable for one-third of the diyah. Then if Zayd's act was intentional, the diyah is due from his own wealth; otherwise, it is upon the 'aqilah as known in its place. And in al-Mabsut and elsewhere: Participation in killing is either that the killer is joined by one whose act is not liable (ghayr madmin) or by one whose act is liable. If joined by one whose act is not liable, like a predatory animal, an animal, a harbi, a apostate, or a person wounds himself then another wounds him, or the imam cuts the hand of a thief in a theft then another cuts his hand, or wounds him and he dies, then there is no qisas upon the killer by consensus. If joined by one whose act is liable, like the mistaken one, a boy, or an insane person, then there is no qisas upon either of them, and if it were intentional instead of mistaken, then one diyah is due. And if two men wound him intentionally, then one of the wounders dies, then the wounded dies, or two men shoot at another, one of them dies, then two arrows hit and he dies from that, is qisas due upon the living? Some said: yes, because the act of each one is causative. Some said: no, because the act of one only becomes causative after the injury, so one does not become causative by itself. Two men kill a man, one with a sword and the other with a stick: the diyah is upon the 'aqilah of the one with the stick, and qisas upon the one with the sword. And in al-Mabsut: The principle is that whenever a life is lost by multiple injuries and money is required, then we look: If it is lost by injuries from human beings, then consideration is of the number of perpetrators, not the number of injuries, in terms of liability; so if one person inflicted ten wounds by mistake and another inflicted one wound by mistake, the diyah is half and half upon them, because the act of a human being is considered in itself, as it does not change its ruling in this world (qisas or diyah) or sin in the Hereafter, so the number of perpetrators is considered, not the number of injuries, because each injury could be the cause of death if alone, and the cause is not strengthened by an increase of its own kind, so all are considered one injury. And if the life is lost by injuries from animals and injuries from humans, then the number of injuries is not considered because the act of animals is absolutely worthless, as no ruling is attached to it, so all animal injuries are considered one injury, because the ruling of all is one: worthless. This is like a man with wounds and boils (that are fatal), then another man wounds him and he dies from all, the wounder is liable for half the diyah and half is dropped and the number of boils is not considered because they are worthless. And if a man cuts his hand, and another throws a stone on him, and a dog bites his leg, and a predatory animal attacks him, then the cutter is liable for half the diyah, because the life is lost by four injuries, one from a human (the cutter) which is considered, and the rest are worthless, so it is as if it was lost by two injuries, one considered and one worthless. And if a man cuts his hand, another wounds him, and he himself wounds himself, and a predatory animal attacks him, then the cutter is liable for a quarter of the diyah and the wounder for a quarter, because the life is lost by four injuries, two of which are from humans and are considered, and one from a non-human (the animal) which is worthless, so it is lost by the injury of each of the two outsiders (ajnabiyayn) by a quarter. And its explanation has preceded.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage discusses liability when death results from multiple causes. The scenario involves four factors: the victim's own action (self-harm), Zayd's action, a lion, and a snake. The text explains how to apportion diyah. The lion and snake acts are 'hadar' (valueless in terms of liability) in both worlds; the victim's own action is hadar in this world but sinful in the next; Zayd's action is fully considered in both worlds. Thus there are three categories: absolutely worthless (lion, snake), relatively worthless (self), and fully considerable (Zayd). Therefore Zayd is liable for half the diyah (but earlier it says half, then explains one-third? Actual calculation: They are three categories, but the text says 'thabat fi'lan wahidan' meaning the two worthless categories count as one act, so total acts = 2, Zayd's act = 1, so Zayd pays half? The translation says halfthen explains thabul diyah? In the OCR it says 'ضمن زيد نصف الدية' then later 'فصارت ثلاثة أجناس : هدر مطلقاً، الثابت فعلاً واحداً فيجب على زيد ثلث الدية' So there is inconsistency: text says half, then sharh says one-third. Maybe the printed text has 'ثلث' but OCR gave 'نصف'? The OCR clearly says 'نصف الدية'. So the matn says half, but the sharh says one-third? Actually the sharh says 'فوجب على زيد ثلث الدية'. This may be a scribal error. As a commentator, we note this. The passage then goes into general principles from al-Mabsut about multiple participants in killing. It categorizes participants into those whose acts are liable (mu'tabar) and those not liable (hādir). It gives examples: if a lion and a man participate, no qisas on the man; if two liable participants (e.g., two humans), they share diyah equally if both mistaken, but if one intentional and one mistaken, the intentional one faces qisas and the mistaken one pays diyah via 'aqilah. The principle is: when multiple human acts contribute, liability is divided equally among the number of human perpetrators, not the number of injuries. Animal acts are ignored entirely.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Worthless; not entailing any criminal or civil liability in this world.
معتبر (mu'tabar): Considered; legally relevant for liability (diyah, qisas).
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of male relatives of the killer who pay diyah on his behalf for unintentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the original scenario (death by self, Zayd, lion, snake), what is Zayd's liability per the matn?
A) Full diyah.
B) Half diyah.
C) One-third diyah.
D) No diyah.

Key Takeaway
When death results from multiple causes, Hanafi law divides diyah by the number of liable human perpetrators, treating animal acts and self-harm as a single worthless cause.

Drawing a Weapon Against Muslims – Justified Lethal Defense
Ruling with proof from hadith and legal analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a person who brandishes a weapon against Muslims, identify the supporting hadith, and explain why no liability falls on the defender.

Core Idea
A weapon-brandisher against Muslims becomes licit to kill; the defender bears no liability.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical scenario: a person draws a sword and threatens Muslims. The Hanafi school, based on a prophetic hadith and the principle of repelling harm, rules that such an aggressor may be killed, and the killer incurs no legal consequences. We will unpack the ruling, its proof, and a minor intra-madhhab difference regarding funeral rites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The author states: 'Whoever draws a sword against Muslims, his killing becomes obligatory and nothing is due for killing him.' Note the hukm (wajib) and the muta'allaq (the aggressor).
The act of shahr al-silah triggers this ruling.
2
Locate the primary evidence (dalil naqli)
The author quotes the hadith: 'Whoever draws a sword against Muslims has made his blood permissible.' Understand how this serves as the direct proof.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in practice.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text adds: 'repelling harm is obligatory' (daf' al-darar wajib). If killing is the only way to repel, it becomes obligatory. The aggressor is classified as a baghi (rebel), whose blood is licit.
This is a qiyas from the general principle of daf' al-darar.
4
Examine the scope and exceptions
The ruling applies always—night/day, inside/outside the city—except when help is readily available (fi al-misr naharan), where killing may not be justified. Understand the condition for application.
This shows the principle is restricted by the ability to seek help.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on post-mortem treatment
Al-Nawadir contains two views: one that the slain aggressor is washed and prayed over (like a normal Muslim), another that he is washed but not prayed over. This does not affect the core ruling of no liability.
This ikhtilaf is a secondary detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ومن أشهر على المسلمين سيفاً وجب قتله ولا شيء بقتله ) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «من شهر على المسلمين سيفاً فقد أبطل دمه ولأن دفع الضرر واجب فوجب عليهم قتله إذا لم يكن دفعه إلا به، ولا يجب على القاتل شيء لأنه صار باغياً بذلك، وكذا إذا أشهر على رجل سلاحاً فقتله أو قتله غيره دفعاً عنه فلا يجب بقتله شيء لما بينا، ولا يختلف بين أن يكون بالليل أو بالنهار في المصر أو خارج المصر لأنه لا يلحقه الغوث بالليل ولا في خارج المصر فكان له دفعة بالقتل بخلاف ما إذا كان في المصر نهاراً. وفي النوادر : يغسل ويصلى عليه، وعن الثاني يغسل ولا يصلى عليه.
He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And whoever draws a sword against Muslims, his killing becomes obligatory and nothing is due for killing him.) Because of the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever draws a sword against Muslims has made his blood permissible.' And because repelling harm is obligatory, so it becomes obligatory upon them to kill him if they cannot repel it except by that. And nothing is due upon the killer because he has become a rebel (baghi) by that. Similarly, if he draws a weapon against a man, and the man kills him or someone else kills him in defense, then nothing is due for killing him, as we have explained. And there is no difference between night and day, in the city or outside the city, because help cannot reach him at night nor outside the city, so it is permissible to kill him in defense, unlike if it is in the city during the day. And in al-Nawadir: he is washed and prayed upon (like a normal muslim). And from another transmission: he is washed but not prayed upon.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on a person who brandishes a sword against Muslims (or a specific person) in a threatening manner. The hadith states that such a person's blood is licit (abtala damahu). The legal reasoning is that repelling harm (daf' al-darar) is wajib, and if killing is the only way, it becomes obligatory. The killer incurs no liability because the aggressor becomes a baghi (rebel). The rule applies whether it is night or day, inside or outside the city, because the victim cannot get help at night or outside. However, if it is inside the city during the day, help may be possible, so the ruling might differ (as indicated by 'bikhilaf ma idha kana fi al-misr naharan'). The text also notes a difference from al-Nawadir: whether the slain aggressor is washed and prayed over. One view says yes (like a normal Muslim), another says washed but not prayed over.

Key Terms
شهر السلاح (shahr al-silah): Brandishing or drawing a weapon in a threatening manner.
باغ (baghi): Rebel; one who unjustly attacks Muslims, whose blood becomes licit.
دفع الضرر (daf' al-darar): Repelling harm; a legal principle that makes it obligatory to prevent harm to oneself or others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, what is the status of a person who draws a sword against Muslims?
A) He must be arrested and tried.
B) His blood becomes licit (permissible to kill).
C) He must pay diyah if killed.
D) Only the ruler can execute him.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits killing a person who draws a weapon against Muslims without liability, based on the hadith and the principle of repelling harm.

Drawing a Weapon Against an Individual – No Liability for Defender
Ruling on killing a person who brandishes a weapon against an individual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a person who kills an aggressor brandishing a weapon or a stick is not liable, applying the distinctions of time and place.

Core Idea
Lethal self-defense against a weapon is always justified; against a stick, only outside the city.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school: when a person threatens another with a weapon or even a stick, the threatened individual may kill the aggressor without facing legal liability. However, the ruling contains important conditions based on the type of object, time, and location. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying the law correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the aggressor's action
Determine whether the aggressor brandished a weapon (silah) or a stick ('asa). The ruling differs based on the object.
Weapons include swords, knives, guns; sticks are considered lesser threats.
2
Assess time and place
For a weapon, time and place are irrelevant. For a stick, check if the incident occurred at night or outside the city (fi ghayrihi). Inside the city by day, a stick does not justify lethal force.
The commentary explains that inside the city by day, help is available, so killing a stick-wielder is not permitted.
3
Apply the ruling
If the conditions are met (weapon anytime/anywhere, or stick at night/outside city), then the victim may kill the aggressor without any liability (la daman).
The ruling states 'fala shay'a alayhi' – nothing is upon him.
4
Recognize the limitation
The passage begins a new sub-ruling about wounding (fashajjahu) but is cut off. Note that the text is incomplete, but the core ruling is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ومن شهر على رجل سلاحاً ليلاً أو نهاراً في المصر أو غيره أو شهر عليه عصا ليلاً أو نهاراً في غيره فقتله المشهور عليه فلا شيء عليه) فشجه رحمه الله :
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And whoever brandishes a weapon against a man, by night or by day, in the city or elsewhere, or brandishes a stick against him by night or by day elsewhere, and the one against whom it is brandished kills him, then there is nothing upon him.) Then he wounded him, may Allah have mercy on him: ...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage states a rule similar to the previous one but specifically for a threat against an individual (not the general Muslim public). The aggressor brandishes a weapon (silah) or even a stick ('asa) by night or day, but if in the city during the day, it may be different. The condition 'fi ghayrihi' (elsewhere) for the stick indicates that inside the city, a stick may not justify killing because help is available. The victim (or another) may kill the aggressor without liability. The text then breaks off with 'fashajjahu' (and he wounded him), which suggests a new ruling about wounding, but it is cut off.

Key Terms
سلاح (silah): Weapon; any instrument capable of killing, such as sword, knife, gun.
عصا ('asa): Stick; considered a lesser threat than a weapon, so its use only justifies killing outside the city at night.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is it permissible to kill a person who brandishes a stick at another person without liability?
A) Always.
B) Only if it is night and outside the city.
C) Only if in the city.
D) Only if the stick is made of iron.

Key Takeaway
Killing an aggressor who brandishes a weapon is always liability-free; for a stick, liability is avoided only if the attack occurs at night or outside the city.

Killing a Stick-Brandisher: When Qisas Applies
Ruling on killing a stick-brandisher in a city daytime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a stick-brandisher in a city during daytime and explain the three opinions within the school, identifying the operative fatwa according to later authorities.

Core Idea
If help is available, killing a stick-brandisher is transgression; if help is absent, it may be self-defense.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a scenario from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: a person brandishes a stick at another in a populous city during daytime, and the threatened person kills the brandisher. The ruling hinges on the nature of the stick and the availability of public assistance. We will explore the three Hanafi positions and understand how context changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the default ruling
Identify the components: a stick (not a weapon), daytime, city (help available). The base ruling is that the killer faces qisas because the killing was unnecessary aggression.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa considers the stick light (not lethal) and help available, so the killer had non-lethal alternatives. Therefore, the killing is transgression warranting qisas.
The stick is not like a weapon in his view.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the stick is akin to a weapon (since it can kill), so the killing could be intentional murder; they also require qisas but for a different reason.
The difference is theoretical but outcome same in this case.
4
Understand the later opinion (contemporary fatwa)
The text reports a shift: if people no longer rush to help, the killer may have no liability because self-defense becomes necessary. This view is considered for fatwa in later times.
Relies on change in 'urf (custom).
5
Determine the operative ruling
Although the text does not explicitly choose, later Hanafi fatwa often adopts the contemporary opinion, especially in urban settings where help is unreliable.
This shows how fiqh adapts to social change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شهر عصا نهاراً في مصر فقتله المشهور عليه قتل به لأن العصا خفيفة والغوث غير منقطع في المصر فكان بالقتل معتدياً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى ظاهر لأنه ليس كالسلاح عنده ، وقيل عندهما يحتمل أن يكون على الخلاف المذكور في العمد لأنه كالسلاح عندهما حتى يجب القصاص بالقتل به وقد بيناه، وقيل هذا في الزمان المتقدم، أما اليوم إذا شهر عليه العصا في مصر وقتله لا شيء عليه لأن الناس تركوا الإغاثة والغوث.
And whoever brandishes a stick in a city during the daytime, and the one against whom it is brandished kills him, he is executed for it, because the stick is light and help is not cut off in the city, so he is an aggressor by killing. This is apparent according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, because for him it is not like a weapon. And it is said that according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it may be based on the difference mentioned in intentional killing because it is like a weapon according to them, so qisas is obligatory by killing with it, and we have explained this. And it is said that this (ruling) is in earlier times; but today, if one brandishes a stick against him in the city and kills him, there is nothing upon him because people have abandoned assistance and help.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the original matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) with commentary expansion. It addresses the scenario where someone brandishes a stick (not a weapon) against another in a city during daytime, and the threatened person kills the brandisher. The ruling according to Abu Hanifa is that the killer is subject to qisas (retributive execution) because the stick is not a lethal weapon and help (from others) is readily available in the city, so the killing is an act of aggression without necessity. The commentary notes a reported position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): they consider the stick akin to a weapon, and thus the killing would be considered intentional murder warranting qisas. However, a later opinion suggests that in contemporary times (i.e., the author's era) when people no longer rush to help, the killer incurs no liability because self-defense requires lethal force. The passage highlights the importance of contextual factors (availability of help) in determining self-defense claims.

Key Terms
شهر العصا (shahr al-‘asā): Brandishing a stick as a means of intimidation or threat.
الغوث (al-ghawth): Help or assistance from others, which is assumed to be available in a populous city.
القصاص (al-qisās): Retaliatory punishment equal to the crime, specifically execution for murder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later opinion presented in this passage, why would a person who kills a stick-brandisher in a city today have no liability?
A) Because sticks are not considered weapons in any era.
B) Because people have abandoned helping others, so self-defense becomes necessary.
C) Because the city environment always provides safe escape routes.
D) Because the ruler has abolished qisas for such cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on killing an attacker depends on the availability of help; when help is absent, the killing may be justified self-defense.

Killing a Madman, Child, or Animal in Self-Defense: The Hanafi Ruling on Diyah
Inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for killing incapacitated attackers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah when a madman, child, or animal is killed in self-defense, and explain the difference from the Shafi'i position and the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A madman's inviolability remains, so killing him in self-defense requires diyah, not qisas.

Self-defense is a valid justification for killing, but what if the attacker lacks full legal capacity, such as a madman, a child, or an animal? In this lesson, we examine a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools, and even within the Hanafi school, regarding who must pay blood money (diyah) and why. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the Hanafi relied-upon position holds diyah wajib, because the attacker's inviolability (ismah) does not drop due to lack of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key act and the three categories of attackers
The passage addresses three scenarios: a madman, a child, or an animal brandishes a weapon and the threatened person kills them. Determine the legal issue: does the killer incur diyah?
The matn states diyah is wajib for all three, but with details.
2
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i says no compensation at all, because killing in defense is legitimate, like killing a sane adult aggressor.
The proof: the attacker's own action compels the killing, and the owner of an animal has no special liability.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's intra-Hanafi different view
Abu Yusuf holds that diyah is not wajib for a madman or child, only for the animal (owner pays). He argues that the acts of madman/child are not considered for liability, based on the principle 'al-ʿajmāʾ jubar' and analogy with a slave.
His school: jinaayat al-ʿajmaaʾ is inconsequential except for the owner.
4
Explain the relied-upon Hanafi position and its reasoning
The text uses 'ولنا أن' to present the Hanafi argument: the act of these entities is not sinful (lack of capacity), so their inviolability does not drop. Thus qisas would technically be due, but the necessity (dafʿ al-sharr) removes qisas, leaving diyah.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and is mufta bihi.
5
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical consequence: In a Hanafi court, the killer (or animal owner) pays diyah. In a Shafi'i court, no compensation at all. Abu Yusuf would impose diyah only for the animal.
This shows how ikhtilaf affects real judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شهر المجنون على غيره سلاحا فقتله المشهور عليه عمدا تجب الدية) وعلى هذا الصبي والدابة وعن أبي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى : لا تجب الدية في الصبي والمجنون. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : لا يجب الضمان في الكل لأنه قتله دفاعاً فصار كالبالغ العاقل وهذا لأنه يصير محمولاً على قتله بفعله كان قال له اقتلني وإلا قتلتك وكون الدابة مملوكة للغير لا تأثير له في وجوب الضمان كالعبد إذا شهر سيفاً على رجل فقتله فإنه لا يجب الضمان فكذا هذا فصار كالعبد إذا صال على الحر فقتله. ولأبي يوسف إن فعل الصبي والمجنون معتبر أصلاً حتى لا يعتبر في حق وجوب الضمان لأن جناية العجماء جبار وكذا عصمتها لحقها، وعصمة الدابة لحق المالك فكان فعلهما مسقطاً لحقهما لعصمتهما فلا يضمنان ويضمن الدابة بخلاف الصيد إذا صال على المحرم أو صيد الحرم على الحلال لأن الشارع أذن في قتله ولم يوجب علينا تحمل أذاه ألا ترى أن الخمس الفواسق أباح قتلها مطلقاً الأذى منها فما ظنك إذا تحقق الأذى ومالك الدابة لم يأذن فيجب الضمان؟ وكذا عصمة عبد الغير الحق نفسه وفعله محظور فتسقط به عصمته. ولنا أن الفعل من هذه الأشياء غير متصف بالحرمة فلم يقع بغياً فلا تسقط العصمة به لعدم الاختيار الصحيح ولهذا يجب القصاص على الصبي والمجنون بقتلهما، فإذا لم تسقط كان قضيته أن يجب القصاص لأنه قتل معصومة إلا أنه لا يجب القصاص لوجود المبيح وهو دفع الشر فتجب الدية.
And if a madman brandishes a weapon against another, and the one against whom it is brandished kills him intentionally, diyah is obligatory. And the same applies to a child and an animal. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him: diyah is not obligatory in the case of a child and a madman. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: compensation is not obligatory in all (these cases) because he killed him in defense, so it becomes like (killing) a sane adult. This is because he is considered as compelled to kill him by his action, as if he said to him: 'Kill me or I will kill you.' And the fact that the animal is owned by someone else has no effect on the obligation of compensation, just as when a slave brandishes a sword against a man and kills him, there is no compensation; so likewise here, it becomes like a slave who attacks a free man and kills him. And for Abu Yusuf, the action of a child and a madman is considered in principle, even if not considered regarding the obligation of compensation because the injury of a dumb animal is of no account (jubar), and similarly its inviolability is for its own right, while the inviolability of an animal is for the right of the owner; so their action drops (the obligation) regarding their own inviolability, so they are not liable (for diyah), but the owner of the animal is liable. Unlike game that attacks a pilgrim (muhrim) or game of the sanctuary (haram) that attacks a non-pilgrim (halal), because the Lawgiver permitted killing it and did not require us to bear its harm; do you not see that the five noxious animals (al-fawasiq al-khams) are permitted to be killed absolutely due to the harm from them? So what do you think when harm is realized, and the owner of the animal did not permit (the attack), so compensation is obligatory? And similarly the inviolability of a slave owned by another is for his own right, but his action is prohibited, so his inviolability drops by it. And for us (the Hanafi position): the action of these entities (madman, child, animal) is not characterized by unlawfulness, so it does not occur as transgression, thus inviolability does not drop due to lack of sound choice. For this reason, qisas is obligatory on a child and a madman if they (intentionally) kill someone. But since inviolability does not drop, the required (qat' al-hukm) is that qisas should be obligatory because he killed a protected person; however, qisas is not obligatory due to the presence of a permissible factor, namely repelling evil, so diyah becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation of diyah when a person kills a madman, child, or animal that is threatening them with a weapon. The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that diyah is wajib in such cases. The commentary presents: (1) The Hanafi position as per Abu Hanifa: diyah is wajib for killing a madman or child, and for an animal, the owner is liable. (2) A narration from Abu Yusuf: no diyah on the child or madman — only on the animal's owner. (3) Imam al-Shafi'i's position: no compensation at all in all cases because it is a valid act of self-defense, similar to killing a sane adult aggressor. The commentary then argues for the Hanafi position: the act of the madman/child/animal is not sinful (because they lack full capacity), so their inviolability does not drop; thus the killer would have committed killing of a protected being. However, due to the necessity of repelling harm (دفع الشر), qisas is not applied, but monetary compensation (diyah) remains wajib. The discussion includes analogies with slaves and animals, and rebuttals to Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf using the principles of jinaayat al-ʿajmaaʾ (injuries by animals) and the concept of protection (ʿismah).

Key Terms
العجماء (al-ʿajmāʾ): A dumb animal; its injury is considered inconsequential (jubar) in terms of liability.
عصمة (ʿiṣmah): Inviolability or protected status of a person or thing, giving rise to liability if violated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position (as presented in the text), what is the ruling when someone kills a madman who is attacking him with a weapon?
A) The killer is exempt from any liability.
B) The killer must pay diyah (blood money).
C) The killer must undergo qisas (execution).
D) The killer must pay half diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, killing a madman, child, or animal in self-defense requires diyah because their inviolability remains, unlike a sane aggressor.

Killing After Withdrawal: Restoration of Inviolability
Ruling with rational proof on restoration of inviolability after withdrawal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an aggressor withdraws after striking and explain the rational basis for the restoration of inviolability.

Core Idea
Killing an aggressor after withdrawal is murder; inviolability is restored once the threat ceases.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a crucial boundary in the law of self-defense: the moment the aggressor withdraws, his blood becomes protected again. Even if he had previously attacked, the right to kill him is limited to the duration of the active threat. Understanding this helps prevent the misuse of self-defense claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage presents a specific case: a brandisher strikes, then withdraws (no longer threatening), and then the victim kills him. What is the sequence of events?
The withdrawal must be genuine; the passage says 'in a manner not intending a second blow.'
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the killer (the original victim) is subject to qisas (retributive execution). This is clear from the text: 'qisas is upon him.'
3
Trace the reasoning (istidlal)
The passage provides a rational proof: the permissibility of killing was contingent on the brandishing and striking (the threat). When the aggressor withdraws genuinely, his evil is repelled without killing, so his inviolability (ismah) returns. Therefore, killing him afterwards is unjustified homicide.
The 'illah (cause) is the presence or absence of active threat.
4
Note the condition
The ruling depends on the withdrawal being genuine—intending no second blow. If the aggressor only feigns withdrawal but still poses a threat, the right to self-defense persists.
The passage does not discuss ambiguous withdrawals; later Hanafi works elaborate on ascertaining intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الله تعالى : (ولو ضربه الشاهر فانصرف فقتله الآخر قتل القاتل معناه إذا شهر رجل على رجل سلاحاً فضربه الشاهر فانصرف، ثم إن المضروب وهو المشهور عليه ضرب الضارب وهو الشاهر فقتله فعليه القصاص لأن الشاهر لما انصرف بعد الضرب عاد معصوماً مثل ما كان لأن حل دمه كان باعتبار شهره وضربه، فإذا رجع على وجه لا يريد ضربة ثانياً اندفع شره فلا حاجة إلى قتله لارتفاع شره بدونه فعادت عصمته فإذا قتله بعد ذلك فقد قتل رجلاً معصوماً ظلماً فيجب عليه القصاص.
Allah says: 'And if the brandisher strikes and then withdraws, and then the other kills him, the killer is executed.' Its meaning: When a man brandishes a weapon against another man, and the brandisher strikes and then withdraws, then the one struck (the victim) strikes the striker (the brandisher) and kills him, then qisas is upon him (the killer). Because the brandisher, when he withdrew after striking, became inviolable again as before, because the permissibility of his blood (i.e., the justification to kill him) was contingent upon his brandishing and striking. So when he returns in a manner not intending a second blow, his evil is repelled, and there is no need to kill him because his evil is removed without it. Thus his inviolability returns. Therefore, if he kills him after that, he has killed an inviolable man wrongfully, so qisas is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling from the matn. It explains the case where a person brandishes a weapon and actually strikes the victim, but then withdraws (stops attacking). If the victim then kills the brandisher after the withdrawal, the victim becomes liable for qisas. The reasoning is that the brandisher's inviolability was temporarily suspended during the attack; once he withdraws with no intention to resume, his inviolability is restored, and there is no longer justification for lethal force. Thus, killing him after the withdrawal is unjustified homicide. The commentary underscores that self-defense must be proportionate and cease when the threat ends.

Key Terms
انصراف (insirāf): Withdrawal or turning away from the attack, indicating cessation of threat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person brandishes a weapon, strikes the victim once, and then withdraws, and the victim then kills him, what is the ruling?
A) The victim is exempt because it is self-defense.
B) The victim must pay diyah.
C) The victim is subject to qisas.
D) The victim must pay half diyah.

Key Takeaway
Killing an aggressor after genuine withdrawal is murder; the right to self-defense is limited to the duration of the active threat.

Killing a Nocturnal Thief: Conditions for Permissibility
Ruling with proof on permissibility of killing a nocturnal thief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which killing a nocturnal thief is permissible without liability and evaluate the exception when shouting is effective.

Core Idea
Killing a fleeing thief is permissible only when recovery of property is impossible through lesser means.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a thief enters a house at night and steals property; the owner pursues and kills him. When is this killing lawful? The Hanafi school answers using the hadith 'Fight to protect your property' and a key logical distinction between the thief and the usurper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Killing a thief who enters at night and flees. The ruling is that there is no liability (no qisas, no diyah).
The default ruling is permissibility (mubah) under conditions.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith 'قاتل دون مالك' (Fight to protect your property). The imperative 'قاتل' indicates permissibility of killing in defense of property.
This is a zanni al-thubut/zanni al-dalalah hadith.
3
Determine the operating condition
The permissibility hinges on the owner's inability to recover the property through lesser means. The text states: 'if he is unable to take it from him'.
Lesser means include shouting, seeking help from Muslims or the judge.
4
Identify the exception
If the owner knows that shouting would cause the thief to drop the property, but kills him anyway, qisas becomes obligatory. Intent and feasibility matter.
This is compared to ghasb (usurpation) where help is available, so killing is not allowed.
5
Compare with ghasb
In ghasb, the owner can seek legal recourse (judge, Muslims), so killing the usurper is murder. The thief's case is different because help may be unavailable at night.
The distinction rests on the possibility of recovery through non-lethal means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن دخل عليه غيره ليلا فاخرج السرقة فاتبعه فقتله فلا شيء عليه لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام قاتل دون مالك» أي لأجل مالك، ولأن له أن يمنعه بالقتل ابتداء فكذا له يسترده به انتهاء إذا لم يقدر على أخذه منه، ولو علم أنه لو صاح عليه يطرح ماله فقتله مع ذلك يجب عليه القصاص لأن قتله بغير حق وهو بمنزلة المغصوب منه إذا قتل الغاصب حيث يجب عليه القصاص لأنه يقدر على دفعه بالاستعانة بالمسلمين والقاضي فلا تسقط عصمته بخلاف السارق والذي لا يندفع بالصياح والله تعالى أعلم.
And (if) someone enters upon another at night and takes out stolen property, and he (the owner) follows him and kills him, there is nothing upon him. Due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Fight to protect your property,' i.e., for the sake of your property. And because he has the right to prevent him (the thief) by killing initially, so likewise he has the right to recover (the property) by killing finally if he is unable to take it from him. And if he knows that if he shouts at him, he would drop the property, but he kills him anyway, then qisas is obligatory upon him, because he killed him without right. And this is similar to the case of a person whose property is usurped (maghsub minhu) when he kills the usurper (ghasib) — qisas is obligatory upon him because he is able to repel him with the help of Muslims and the judge, so his inviolability does not drop. Unlike the thief and the one who cannot be repelled by shouting — and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof. It deals with the case of a thief who enters a house at night (a burglary) and steals property; the owner pursues him and kills him. The ruling is that there is no liability (no qisas, no diyah) because the owner is protecting his property in a situation where he cannot otherwise recover it. The proof is the hadith 'قاتل دون مالك' (fight to protect your property). The commentary explains that just as one may kill at the initial stage to prevent theft, one may also kill in pursuit if there is no other way to retrieve the property. However, an exception is made: if the owner knows that shouting would cause the thief to drop the property, yet kills him anyway, then qisas becomes wajib. This is compared to the case of usurpation (ghasb) where the owner can seek help from authorities; killing is not justified since help is available. Thus, the key factor is the inability to recover property through lesser means.

Key Terms
دون مالك (dūna mālik): In defense of one's property; indicates the cause or purpose for which fighting is permitted.
سارق (sāriq): Thief; one who stealthily takes property.
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper; one who openly takes property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does killing a nocturnal thief incur qisas?
A) When the thief enters during the day.
B) When the owner knows that shouting would make the thief drop the property, yet kills him anyway.
C) When the thief is armed.
D) When the property is recovered after the killing.

Key Takeaway
Killing a fleeing nocturnal thief is permissible only when the owner cannot recover property by lesser means; qisas applies if shouting would have sufficed.

Compensation for a Moved Tooth: Color, Function, and Visibility
States ruling for a tooth that is hit and moves, with waiting period and color-based compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on compensation for a moved tooth based on its color change and type, and apply the preferred position to a given scenario.

Core Idea
For a tooth injury, compensation depends on color, visibility, and functional loss—balancing utility and dignity.

This lesson addresses the Hanafi ruling when a person's tooth is struck and becomes loose. The classical texts prescribe a one-year waiting period to observe color changes. The compensation amount—whether full diyah or a just assessment—depends on the color and type of tooth, reflecting the school's nuanced approach to bodily injuries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage begins by stating the basic rule: when a tooth is struck and moves, one waits a full year. After that, if the tooth turns red, green, or black, full diyah is due. If it turns yellow, there is a disagreement.
The waiting period is to determine the final outcome of the injury.
2
Analyze the color categories
Notice that red, green, and black trigger full diyah without further qualification. Yellow, however, is contested—some say full diyah, others say just a government appraisal (ḥukūmat ʿadl).
This shows that the jurists consider yellow less severe than black/red/green.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi narrations
The passage reports multiple views: Abu Yusuf says full compensation for yellow; Muhammad looks at the degree of disfigurement; Abu Hanifa says ḥukūmat ʿadl; a narration from Hisham says nothing for a free man's yellow tooth but ḥukūmat for a slave.
These differences stem from varying assessments of whether yellowness constitutes significant disfigurement.
4
Apply the preferred detailed distinction
The preferred approach (suggested by later scholars) distinguishes between molars (ḍirās) and incisors (qawāriḍ). For molars, full compensation for blackness only if the function of chewing is lost; otherwise ḥukūmat. For incisors, full compensation for blackness even if function remains, because they are visible and cosmetic damage matters.
This distinction was not made by Muhammad's original statement, but is advocated by the commentators.
5
Determine the muftā bihī
The detailed distinction (molar vs. incisor) appears to be the relied-upon position in the later Ḥanafī school, as it is presented without rejection and resolves the earlier conflict.
The author (Ibn Nujaym) implicitly favors this view by presenting it as the answer of 'they said'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ضرب سن انسان فتحرك ينتظر فيه حولاً فإن احمر أو اخضر أو اسود تجب الدية كاملة في مال الجاني، وإن اصفر اختلف المشايخ فيه؛ هكذا ذكر شيخ الاسلام في شرحه . قال بعضهم : يجب كمال أرش السن كما في الأسود والأحمر. وقال بعضهم يجب حكومة عدل. وذكر شيخ الإسلام أحمد الطواويسي في شرحه أن في هذا الفصل اختلاف الروايات، وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه يلزمه كمال الأرش كما في الأسود، وعن محمد أنه قال ينظر في ذلك، فإن كان يلحقه من الشين بسبب الاصفرار ما يلحقه من الشين بسبب الاسوداد يلزمه كمال الأرش وإلا فيقدر الشين. وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يلزمه حكومة عدل. وذكر القدوري أن هشاماً روى عن محمد عن أبي حنيفة أن سن الحر إذا اصفرت فلا شيء، وإن كان عبداً ففيه حكومة عدل، وعن أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة أن فيه الحكومة، وروي عن أبي مالك عن أبي يوسف أن الصفرة إذا اشتدت حتى صارت كالخضرة ففيها كمال الأرش، وإن كانت دون ذلك ففيها الحكومة. ثم إن محمداً أوجب كمال الأرش باسوداد السن ولم يفصل بين يكون السن من الأضراس التي لا ترى أو من القوارض التي ترى قالوا: ويجب أن يكون الجواب فيها على التفصيل إن كان السن من الأضراس التي لا ترى إن فاتت منفعة المضغ بالأسوداد يجب الأرش كاملاً، وإن لم تفت متفعة المضغ يجب فيه حكومة عدل، وإن كان السن قائمة من القوارض التي ترى وتظهر من الأسنان فيجب كمال الأرش بالاسوداد وإن لم تفت منفعته.
And when a person's tooth is struck and it moves, one waits for a year. If it becomes red, green, or black, the full diyah is obligatory from the property of the aggressor. If it becomes yellow, the scholars differed on it. Thus Sheikh al-Islam mentioned in his commentary. Some said: the full compensation of the tooth is obligatory as in the black and red. And some said: a just assessment is obligatory. And Sheikh al-Islam Ahmad al-Tawawisi mentioned in his commentary that in this chapter there is a difference of transmissions. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he must pay the full compensation as in the black. From Muhammad that he said: one looks into it: if the disfigurement due to yellowness is like the disfigurement due to blackness, the full compensation is obligatory; otherwise the disfigurement is assessed. From Abu Hanifa that a just assessment is obligatory. And al-Quduri mentioned that Hisham narrated from Muhammad from Abu Hanifa that if the free man's tooth becomes yellow, nothing is due; if it is a slave, then a just assessment. From Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa: that there is an assessment. And it is narrated from Abu Malik from Abu Yusuf: if yellowness intensifies until it becomes like greenness, the full compensation is due; if it is less than that, then an assessment. Then Muhammad made full compensation obligatory due to blackening of the tooth and did not distinguish whether the tooth is a molar that is not seen or an incisor that is seen. They said: the answer must be detailed: if the tooth is a molar that is not seen, if the benefit of chewing is lost by blackness, the full compensation is due; if the benefit of chewing is not lost, a just assessment is due. If the tooth is an incisor that is seen and apparent among teeth, then full compensation is due by blackness, even if its benefit is not lost.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute about compensation for a tooth that is struck and moves. The author presents the general rule: wait one year for a moved tooth. If it turns red, green, or black, full diyah is due. If yellow, there is a disagreement. Sheikh al-Islam reports that some say full compensation like black/red, others say just assessment (hukumat adl). Then the author details different narrations from Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Abu Hanifa. A key distinction is whether the tooth is a hidden molar or a visible incisor. Molars: full compensation only if chewing function is lost; incisors: full compensation due to blackness even if function intact, because appearance matters. This shows the Hanafi school's nuanced approach considering both function and cosmetic damage.

Key Terms
حكومة عدل (hukumat adl): A monetary assessment determined by a just expert, not a fixed amount, for injuries where the specific compensation is not prescribed.
الأرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for injuries to specific body parts, e.g., full diyah for a tooth (500 dinars in some narrations).
السن (al-sinn): Tooth; in this context, any tooth, but later distinguished as molar (ضرس) or incisor (قارضة).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred detailed view, a blackened incisor that still functions normally requires:
A) Nothing, because function is intact
B) Full diyah, because it is a visible tooth
C) Hukumat adl, because it is not a molar
D) Half diyah, because function is intact

Key Takeaway
Compensation for a moved tooth in Hanafi law is not uniform; it depends on color (red/green/black vs. yellow), tooth type (molar vs. incisor), and functional loss.

Diyah Calculation for All 32 Teeth in Hanafi Fiqh
Calculation of diyah for all 32 teeth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total diyah for multiple tooth extractions using the Hanafi proportional method and identify the three-year payment schedule.

Core Idea
When all 32 teeth are knocked out, diyah is 1 and 3/5 of the full diyah, not just one.

The Hanafi school specifies detailed compensation amounts for bodily injuries. For teeth, a single tooth's diyah is often 1/20 of full diyah, but when all 32 teeth are lost, a proportional calculation yields a total of 8/5 diyah. This lesson parses the text from Sharh al-Tahawi to understand the math and payment structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tooth categories
List the four types of teeth mentioned: 20 molar (أضراس), 4 canines (أنياب), 4 incisors (ثنايا), and 4 premolars (ضواحك). Total 32 teeth.
These are the standard adult teeth.
2
Recognize the diyah ratio for each group
The text implies that 20 molars constitute one full diyah, and the remaining 12 teeth (4+4+4) constitute 3/5 of a diyah. This ratio (12/20 = 3/5) is used to calculate the additional compensation.
The principle: compensation is proportional to the number of teeth in the full set of 20 (which equals one diyah).
3
Compute the total diyah fraction
Add 1 (full diyah for molars) + 3/5 = 8/5 diyah. This is the total compensation due.
In dirhams: full diyah = 12,000 dirhams, so total = 12,000 × 8/5 = 19,200 dirhams.
4
Interpret the payment schedule
The text mentions payment over three years. The first year includes two-thirds of the total? Actually, the garbled text says 'Six in the first year: two-thirds of the diyah? One-third of the full diyah and one-third of three-fifths.' This likely means the first year payment is 1/3 of the full diyah + 1/3 of (3/5 diyah) = 1/3 + 1/5 = 8/15 of the full diyah? The second year is another 1/3 of the full diyah, and the third year is the remainder.
The exact fractions are uncertain due to OCR, but the principle is a deferred payment over three years.

Source Text

The diyah for one tooth is often 1/20 of full diyah? But here it says total is diyah + 3/5 diyah = 8/5 diyah. This suggests that the 20 molars count as one diyah, and the other 12 teeth (4 canines + 4 incisors + 4 premolars) count as 3/5 diyah? The payment is spread over three years: in the first year, some portion (maybe 2/3 of total? The text is garbled). The lesson shows the meticulous calculation of fixed compensations for specific body parts.

Tooth Grows Back After Extraction
Ruling on compensation when a tooth grows back after being pulled accidentally or intentionally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation when an extracted tooth regrows naturally, and distinguish between cases where compensation is waived or remains due.

Core Idea
If a pulled tooth grows back naturally, the aggressor pays nothing—compensation is tied to permanent loss.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a detailed ruling on liability when a tooth is extracted and later regrows. The Hanafi school distinguishes between natural regeneration and artificial restoration, and also addresses intra-school differences and a cross-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school regarding the force of analogy versus text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the accidental extraction of a tooth. The ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah: no compensation is due if the tooth grows back naturally. Compensation is wajib only if it does not regrow.
2
Locate the dalil
The authority is a text (hadith) that obligates compensation only when no replacement tooth grows. The text overrides the default qiyas (analogy) which would not require compensation since the tooth is not considered property.
Qiyas alone would deny compensation even if no regrowth, but the text creates an exception.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The text is interpreted restrictively: compensation is linked to the condition of no regrowth. When regrowth occurs, the condition is absent, so the compensation obligation falls away and the original qiyas applies (no compensation).
The Hanafi method: a text that limits (takhsis) the general rule.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) holds that compensation remains even if the tooth regrows, analogizing to cutting a tree: a new tree does not nullify the penalty. Abu Yusuf also differs: for an adult's regrown tooth, full diyah is due; for a child's, a just assessment.
These disputes show the tension between textual interpretation and qiyas.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author (Ibn Nujaym) declares the Zahir al-Riwayah position correct: no compensation upon natural regrowth. This is the mufta bihi because it rests on both the explicit text and a sound distinction (the regrown tooth is the same substance recreated by Allah, unlike a new tree which is a separate entity).
The Hanafi response to Shafi'i: the tree analogy is invalid because the new tree is a different entity; the regrown tooth is the original essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قلع الرجل سن رجل خطأ ثم نبتت فلا شيء على القالع عند علمائنا، وروي عنهما في النوادر أنه يجب الأرش، والصحيح ما قلنا لأن القياس يأبى وجوب الأرش بالقلع وإن لم تنبت لأن المتلف ليس بمال ولكنا تركنا القياس بالنص وإنما أوجب النص الأرش إذا لم تنبت مكانه أخرى فإذا نبتت مكانه أخرى يقع على أصل القياس، فإذا نبتت أخرى سوداء بقي الأرش على حاله . وإذا نزع سن رجل عمداً أو انتزع المنزوع سنه سن النازع ثم نبتت سن الأول فعلى الأول أرش سن الثاني، ولو نبت معوجاً يجب حكومة عدل، وإن نبتت سوداء جعل كأنها لم تنبت . وفي الكافي : ولو قلع سن غيره فردها صاحبها إلى مكانها ونبت عليها اللحم فعلى القالع كمال الأرش. وقال الشافعي في قول عليه الضمان بخلاف ما لو قطع شجرة رجل فنبتت مكانها أخرى حيث لا يسقط الضمان السغنافي ذكر في المبسوط : ولو قلع سن رجل فنبتت كما كانت فلا شيء عليه في ظاهر الرواية ويرجع على الجاني بقدر ما يحتاج إليه من ثمن الدواء وأجرة الأطباء، وأبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى يقول : لا يجب شيء. وفي الينابيع وقال أبو يوسف : لو نبتت سن البالغ بعد القلع لا يسقط الأرش بل تلزمه الدية كاملة بخلاف سن الصبي. وقال أبو حنيفة : لا شيء في سن الصبي. وقال أبو يوسف : فيها حكومة عدل وإذا لم تنبت يجب فيها الأرش كاملاً.
And if a man pulls out another's tooth accidentally, then it grows back, nothing is due from the puller according to our scholars. It is narrated from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in the Nawadir that compensation is obligatory. The correct is what we said, because analogy (qiyas) would not require compensation for extraction even if it does not grow back, since the thing destroyed is not property. But we left analogy due to a text, and the text only made compensation obligatory if no other tooth grows in its place. So if another tooth grows in its place, it is upon the original analogy. If another tooth grows black, the compensation remains as it was. And if a man intentionally pulls out a tooth, or the one whose tooth was pulled pulls out the tooth of the puller, then the first tooth of the first grows back, then the first owes compensation of the second tooth. If it grows crooked, a just assessment is due. If it grows black, it is considered as if it did not grow. And in al-Kafi: If one pulls out another's tooth and the owner puts it back in place and flesh grows over it, then the puller owes full compensation. Al-Shafi'i in one opinion says guarantee is upon him, unlike if a man cuts a tree of another and another tree grows in its place where the guarantee does not fall away. Al-Sighnaqi mentioned in al-Mabsut: If a man pulls out a tooth and it grows back as it was, nothing is due upon him in the apparent narration; and he recovers from the aggressor the cost of medicine and doctor's fees. Abu Hanifa says: nothing is due. And in al-Yanabii', Abu Yusuf said: If an adult's tooth grows back after extraction, the compensation is not dropped, but the full diyah is obligatory, unlike the tooth of a child. Abu Hanifa said: nothing is due for a child's tooth. Abu Yusuf said: there is a just assessment for it. And if it does not grow, full compensation is obligatory.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute about compensation when a tooth grows back after being extracted. The author states the position of our scholars (Hanafi): if a tooth grows back naturally after accidental extraction, no compensation is due. There are narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the Nawadir (rare transmissions) that compensation is obligatory, but the correct view (based on Zahir al-Riwayah) is no compensation. The reasoning: qiyas would not require compensation anyway because a tooth is not property, but a text (hadith) made compensation obligatory only if it does not grow back. If it grows back, the original analogy applies (no compensation). Then he discusses intentional extraction and retaliation: if the victim's tooth grows back after he pulled the aggressor's tooth, he still owes compensation for the aggressor's tooth. If it grows back crooked or black, it affects compensation. The passage also covers replantation by the owner: if the tooth is replanted and flesh grows, full compensation is still due because it did not return to its original state. There is a dispute with Shafi'i, who holds guarantee even if it grows back, analogous to cutting a tree and a new one growing. The Hanafi position distinguishes: for a tree, the new tree is a different entity; for a tooth, it is the same essence recreated. Then details on adult vs. child teeth: Abu Yusuf holds full diyah for adult regrowth, Abu Hanifa nothing for child's tooth, Abu Yusuf says just assessment. The lesson emphasizes that natural regrowth aborts compensation, but replantation does not.

Key Terms
النوادر (al-nawadir): Rare or non-standard transmissions from the founding imams, not part of the six major books (Zahir al-Riwayah).
القياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning: comparing the new case to an established ruling based on a common effective cause ('illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in Zahir al-Riwayah, what is the ruling when an accidentally extracted tooth grows back naturally?
A) Full diyah is due
B) A just assessment (hukumat adl) is due
C) No compensation is due
D) The aggressor must pay for medical expenses only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for tooth extraction is tied to permanent loss: if the tooth regenerates naturally, no compensation is due; artificial restoration does not nullify liability.

Who Speaks First? Tooth Blackening Disputes in Hanafi Law
Procedure when victim claims aggressor caused blackness, aggressor denies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whose word is accepted in a tooth blackening dispute and explain the role of istihsan and the visible effect (athar) in shifting the burden of proof.

Core Idea
In tooth blackening disputes, the victim's word is accepted by istihsan due to the visible effect — an exception to the usual rule that the defendant's word is taken.

In Hanafi criminal law, the general rule is that the defendant's denial is accepted with an oath. However, for a specific injury — blackening of a tooth — the rule is reversed: the victim's claim is preferred. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute and the istihsan reasoning that creates this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural issue
We have a dispute between striker and victim about the cause of tooth blackening. The question is whose statement is accepted as the default.
General rule: defendant's word (striker's denial) is accepted.
2
State the general Hanafi principle
Normally, the person who denies liability (striker) has his word accepted with oath. The plaintiff (victim) must prove causation.
3
Recognize the exceptional case for tooth
For tooth blackening, the rule is reversed: the victim's word is accepted by istihsan.
This exception is unique to tooth injuries.
4
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan departs from qiyas due to the visible blackness (athar) which serves as circumstantial evidence supporting the victim's claim.
The athar makes the victim's claim stronger than usual.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa has a narration that the striker's word is accepted, maintaining the general principle. Abu Yusuf's view is in al-Asl and is preferred.
Both views are valid, but the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا إذا ضرب إنسان إنساناً وأسود السن فقال الضارب إنما اسودت من ضربة حدثت فيها بعد ضربتي فالقول للمضروب استحساناً؛ هكذا ذكر المسألة في الأصل وهكذا روى ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف. وفي المنتقى في الباب الأول من الجنايات رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة في عين هذه الصورة أن القول قول الضارب وليس هذا في شيء من الجنايات إلا في السن للأثر.
And upon this, if a person strikes another and his tooth becomes black, and the striker says: It only became black from a blow that occurred after my strike, then the word is of the struck one by istihsan. Thus the issue is mentioned in al-Asl, and thus Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Abu Yusuf. And in al-Muntaqa, in the first chapter of jinayat, a narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa on the exact same case: that the word is of the striker. And this is not in any of the jinayat (chapters) except in the tooth due to an athar (trace/effect).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute about whose statement is accepted when there is a dispute over the cause of tooth blackening. The general principle (from Abu Yusuf and the Asl) is that the victim's word is taken with oath, by istihsan (juristic preference). However, there is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that the striker's word is taken. The author notes that this exceptional ruling (victim's word) applies only in the case of a tooth, due to a specific effect (athar) — perhaps meaning that blackening is an external sign that supports the victim's claim. The lesson highlights that in most injuries, the defendant's denial is accepted with oath, but for tooth blackening, the victim is preferred because the effect is apparent and likely caused by the blow.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: departing from qiyas due to a stronger evidence or custom. Here, preferring victim's word despite the norm of defendant's word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school makes an exceptional procedural rule for tooth blackening by istihsan, accepting the victim's word over the striker's denial due to the visible effect.
